3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
Relaxed SATA Initialization Extension Enable (Non-ALIGN Detection) (RSIXE): This bit affects the S-ATA initialization sequence
following COMRESET, COMINIT, or COMWAKE events. Specifically, it controls the number of non-ALIGN primitives that the
Host Controller must detect in order to transition from HR_SendAlign state to HR_Ready state (as states are described in S-
ATA spec rev 1.0 in section 6.8.1.1.1).
CTM[112:10] signifies the following non-ALIGN detection scheme:
‘111’ = reserved
12:10 RW 000 ‘110’ = reserved
‘101’ = single non-ALIGN (same as IBX behavior)
‘100’ = reserved
‘011’ = 2 non-consecutive non-ALIGN
‘010’ = 2 consecutive non-ALIGN
‘001’ = 3 non-consecutive non-ALIGN
‘000’ = 3 consecutive non-ALIGN
9 RW 0 Reserved.
AHCI Non-Queue Data-In Undeflow Detection Enable (NQIUFD): When this bit is set to ‘1’, AHCI data-in underflow detection in
non-queue data-in operation can be detected. Upon detection, the DMA engine proceeds to the next Command Issue (CI).
8 RW 0 When this bit is cleared to ‘0’, certain AHCI data-in underflow scenarios may not be detected in non-queue data-in operation
and the DMA engine may hang as a result.
BIOS is required to program this field to ‘1’.
DMA Stops CI Progress Enable (STCI): When this bit is set to ‘1’, subsequent Command List read requests are blocked when
the DMA engine has stopped.
7 RW 0 When this bit is cleared to ‘0’, the DMA engine continues to process the next CI even though the DMA engine may have
stopped due to an error condition or PxTFD.STS.ERR = 1.
BIOS is required to program this field to ‘1’.
PRD Fetch-Ahead Disable (PFAD). When this bit is set, DMA engine will not fetch ahead the next PRD. The fetch of next PRD
will only be initiated when the current PRD is completed. In the case of DMA write, this is when all write data has been flushed
out of SATA host controller to memory. In the case of DMA read, this is when all read completion has been returned to SATA
6 RW 0 host controller. When this bit is cleared, the DMA engine will fetch ahead the next PRD if EOL bit of current PRD is not set and
PRD queue space is available. This could be before current PRD is completed. This bit applies to legacy operation only. It has
no affect on AHCI operation.
The bit should not be changed after START bit set to ‘1’.
CONT Primitive Generation Disable (CPGD). This bit is set to disable the CONT primitive generation from the SATA host
controller. Instead, SATA controller will repeat the primitive itself as allowed by the spec. When this bit is cleared, SATA
5 RW 0
controller will generate CONT primitive for the repeatative primitives.
SATA host controller will always accept CONT primitive regardless of this bit.
AHCI PRD Byte Count Update During AHCI Data-In Underflow Disable (BCUUD): When this bit is set to ‘1’, PRD Byte Count
Update (PRDBC) during AHCI non-queued data-in underflow is omitted.
4 RW 0
When this bit is cleared to ‘0’, the SATA host controller will provide the exact Byte Count update to the PRDBC in the event of
data-in underflow from a SATA device.
Short Data FIS Testmode Select[1:0] (SDFTSEL). This is the select for the short Data FIS testmode. When this field is “00”, the
testmode is not selected and SATA host controller will send Data FISes to the max of 8192 bytes as allowed by the spec. Any
other setting of these bits will cause SATA host controller to break up Data FISes to smaller size for (pre-silicon) testability
purpose.
Value Data FIS Size (bytes)
3:2 RW 00
0 8192
1 32
10 64
11 512
Read Request Size Select[1:0] (RRSSEL). These two bits select the max read request size that SATA host controller will initiate
for DMA read from memory. SATA host controller will internally break up larger read request based on these bits. The request
is address-aligned to the selected size.
BIOS is required to program this field to ‘11’ (64B read request size).
Value Data FIS Size (bytes)
1:0 RW 00
0 256
1 Reserved
10 128
11 64

SATA LPM PLL Shutdown Disable (PLLSHUTDIS): When this bit is set to ‘1’, host controller will not shut down the SATA PLL
due to all ports being in Slumber or equivalent. The only condition to shut down SATA PLL is if all port’s PCS.PxE=‘0’ and/or all
RW /
31 0 / 1 AHCI PxSCTL.DET=Offline.
RO
If SATA PLL shutdown disable Fuse is ‘1’, this register will always read ‘1’ and is Read-only.
If SATA PLL shutdown disable Fuse is ‘0’, the reset value of this register is ‘0’ and is RW.
SATA Common Lane PLL Trunk Clock Gating Disable (PCLKTCGDIS): When this bit is set to ‘1’, host controller will not activate
trunk clock gating on the MOD-PHY Common Lane PLL clock output, due to all ports being in Partial or equivalent. The only
condition to shut down SATA PLL is if all ports being in Slumber or equivalent and PLL is allowed to shut down, or “all port’s
PCS.PxE=‘0’ and/or all AHCI PxSCTL.DET=Offline”. When this bit is ‘0’, host controller will activate trunk clock gating on the
30 RW / 0 / 1 MOD-PHY Common Lane PLL clock output, due to all ports being in Partial or equivalent.
RO
If SATA PCLK Trunk Clock Gating Disable Fuse is ‘1’, this register will always read ‘1’ and is Read-only. If SATA PCLK Trunk
Clock Gating Disable Fuse is ‘0’, the reset value of this register is ‘0’ and is RW.

Implementation note: When this bit is set to ‘0’, clock to the SR-flop is gated at ‘0’ during power down.
SATA IOSF Sideband Dynamic Clock Gating Enable (SIDECLKDCGEN):
29 RW 0 When this bit is set to ‘1’, host controller will activate side_clk dynamic clock gating. Else it is disabled by default.
BIOS is required to program this field to ‘1’.
PLLON BYPASS BACKBONE TRUNK CLOCK UNGATE DISABLE (PLLBPBBTCGDIS):
When this bit is set to “1‟, in the event of device initiated LPM exit, host controller will turn on SATA PLL after Backbone Clock
is ungated and valid.
28 RW 0
When this bit is set to “0”, in the event of device initiated LPM exit, host controller will turn on SATA PLL regardless of
Backbone Clock is valid or not.
This is meant to improve LPM exit latency when BBCLK TCG is active.
Valid GAP Count For Valid COMINIT (VGCVCI):
Number of valid GAP to be qualified as valid COMINIT.
00b - 2 Valid GAP
01b - 3 Valid GAP
10b - 4 Valid GAP
11b - 5 Valid GAP
Note: Prior to ADP_S (with SNPS MP16 PHY) when modPHY was used as HSIO PHY, detection of 3 (hardcode in the RTL) valid
GAP is qualified as valid COMINIT, i.e. valid COMINIT is declared at the beginning of the 4th BURST. This count is
27:26 RW 01b
programmable in ADP_S and onward to improve the survivability of the newly added OOB Filter in PIPE Gasket that pair with
the SNPY MP16 PHY, where the 1st BURST in the COMINIT may get removed by the OOB Filter. 1st BURST Removal is
undesired and it couldhappen due to delayed transition from MASK state (masked phy_mac_rxelecidle to 1) to UNMASK state
(let rxelecidle propagate as is) in the OOB Filter in PIPE Gasket, or other unknown due to new PHY, hence potentially shorthen
the 1st BURST and even the 1st GAP seen by the SATA Controller. The delayed transition is due to the move of OOB Filter from
pre-EB (in ModPHY) to post-EB (in SNPS MP16 PHY) that incur few DW flop stage delay on the rxdata path that is used to
mask/unmask the analog rxelecidle. If 1st BURST get shorthen too much until it’s totally lost then the 1st GAP is seen as
shorthen GAP. If 1st BURST is shorthen but still seen as an unsquelch for at least one rosc_clk then the 1st GAP will not be
missed by SATA Controller.
Port Local RxStandby Power Staggering Enable (PRXPSE):
The CTM2.PPSD must be ‘0’ for this bit to have effect. When PRXPSE=‘1’, SATA local Port RxStandby Power Staggering
mechanism is enabled. Else SATA Host Controller reverts back to previous behavior whereby turning on/off an MOD-PHY RX
25 RW 0 via the dedicated RX control signal (RxStandby) is not guaranteed to be staggered. Two known conditions are during end of
full OOB or when softwares clears multiple PCS.PxE to 0’s.
This bit is to provide survivability in the event of SATA device that do not meet SATA OOB timing specification (theoretically
causing Rx to be turned on simulataneously despite CTM2.PPSD staggering based on power state transition).
24 RO Reserved
SATA PLunit/PLPunit Dynamic Clock Gating Enable (SPLDCGE):
‘0’: SATA PLunit/PLPunit Dynamic clock gating of bb_cclk is disabled
‘1’: SATA PLunit/PLPunit Dynamic clock gating of bb_cclk is enabled
23 RW/RO 0
If SATA Core Clock Dynamic Clock Gating Disable Fuse is “1”, this register will always read “0‟ and is Read-only. The reset
value of this register is”0‟.
BIOS is required to program this field to ‘1’.
SRAM Parity Check Disable (SRAMPARDIS): This bit is applicable to all SATA ports’ SRAM parity check.
22 RW 0
Desktop/mobile BIOS is required to program this field to ‘1’. Server BIOS may leave this field to default ‘0’.
Port Power Staggering Disable (PPSD):
When PPSD=‘1’, SATA local Port Power Staggering mechanism is disabled and SATA Host Controller reverts back to previous
21 RW 0 behavior.
Implementation Note: This bit is only applicable to project that doesn’t have FIA unit integrated. With FIS integrated, the
staggerer in the FIA is used and the staggerer in SATA Host Controller is not applicable.

Dynamic Squelch Detector during LPM Mechanism Disable (DSDLPMD): When DSDLPMD=‘1’, the Dynamic Squelch Detector
Mechanism is disabled during low power state. It means the Squelch Detector circuit is kept in On state during low power
RW / state (similar to pre-ICH10 behavior). This is meant to be a chicken bit for the Dynamic Squelch Detector LPM Mechanism.
20 0 / 1
RO If SATA PHY PM Disable Fuse is ‘1’, this register will always read ‘1’ and is Read-only. If SATA PHY PM Disable Fuse Fuse
is ‘0’, the reset value of this register is ‘0’ and is RW.

Dynamic Squelch Detector Mechanism Disable (DSDD): When DSDD=‘1’, the Dynamic Squelch Detector Mechanism is
disabled during Active state. It means the Squelch Detector circuit is kept in On state during Active state (similar to pre-ICH10
RW / behavior). This is meant to be a chicken bit for the Dynamic Squelch Detector Mechanism.

ISM Extended IDLE Entry Delay Enable (ISMDLYEN):
0 = Disable the extra delay indicated by scale (ISMDLYSCL).
The ISM will wait for ~32 bb clocks (with no interface activity happen within) before entering IDLE state. This is current
18 RW 0
existing behavior.
1 = Enable the extra delay indicated by scale (ISMDLYSCL). This is new behavior.
BIOS is required to program this field to ‘1’.
ISM Extended IDLE Entry Delay Scale (ISMDLYSCL):
0 = When ISMDLYEN is 1, the ISM IDLE entry delay is extended by 4x. The ISM will wait for ~ 32 x 4 bb clocks (with no
17 RW 0 interface activity happen within) before entering IDLE state.
1 = When ISMDLYEN is 1, the ISM IDLE entry delay is extended by 8x. The ISM will wait for ~ 32 x 8 bb clocks (with no
interface activity happen within) before entering IDLE state.
BMU Upstream Post Write Performance Disable (WRPERFDIS):
When set to ‘1’, the performance enhancement is disabled, such that the next req_put of one particular pipe is sequenced after
16 RW 0
the last data phase of previous req_put on the same pipe. When ‘0’, the performance enhancement is enabled, such that the
next req_put of one particular pipe is sequenced after the transaction grant of previous req_put on the same pipe.
Non-ALIGN Primitive Full DW Decode Disable (FNALGNDECDIS):
This bit affects the S-ATA initialization sequence following COMRESET, COMINIT, or COMWAKE events.
15 RW 0 When ‘0’, Link Layer decodes entire DW when looking for non-ALIGN primitive during communication initialization.
When ‘1’, Link Layer only look for K28.3 when looking for non-ALIGN during communication initialization. I.e. same behavior as
CPT-A0.
Dynamic Dual-ALIGN Insertion Scheme (DDAIS) Disable (DDAISD):
“1” = DDAIS is disabled. When disabled, SATA Host Link Layer inserts a dual-ALIGN pair at a fixed interval within every 256 DW
boundary, while the SATA interface is in functional Active state. This is the old behavior in previous SATA SIP.
“0” = DDAIS is enabled in Host Link Layer, of which is designed to reduce the HOLD-HOLDA latency by at least 1 DW. This
14 RW 0
scheme is newly implemented since LPT. DDAISD=‘0’ only has effect when host is transmitting data. Host Link Layer (only if it
is the transmitter) will immediately insert a dual-ALIGN pair upon receiving the first HOLD primitive in a HOLD/HOLDA
sequence, if the received HOLD occurs within 7DW time range from the normal fixed dual-ALIGN insertion point. This early
insertion aims to ensure the subsequent Host’s HOLDA transmission will only be blocked by 1 ALIGN transmission (at most)
that consumes 1DW delay.
13 RW 0 Reserved
ALIGN Detection Watchdog Timer Count (ALDWTC): This field determines the number of clock ticks (in 3.6 us resolution) the
ALIGN detection watchdog timer should count before it expires. Upon timer expiry, SATA host controller shall consider the
interface has squelched and internal PhyRdy shall be deasserted. This value is only meaningful when the setting of UNSQLIND
is 01b. Software must program this field to a value larger than 0 to prevent PhyRdy from being dropped and DRCreset from
12:7 RW 0
being asserted immediately after UNSQLIND is set to 01b.
The host should detect at least one ALIGN primitive at every 256 DW boundary, i.e. about every 3.46 us for Gen2 rate and 6.83
for Gen1 rate. BIOS is required to program this field to a value of at least 4 (i.e. 0x4).
Not for EDS: Value of 4 provide robustness to cater for 1 set of corrupted dual-ALIGN in Gen1 speed.
Unsquelch Indicator (UNSQLIND): This field determines the final unsquelch indicator that should be used to determine if a
device is disconnected. This indicator shall be used in the PhyRdy and DRCreset equations to determine if these signals have
to be de-asserted.
Value Description
Use filtered SATA unsquelch qualified with 8b10b
0
error for device disconnect detection.
Use ALIGN primitive detection method for device
1
6:5 RW 0 disconnect detection.
Reserved. If this value is programmed, the result is
10
undefined.
Reserved. Use filtered SATA unsquelch indication as it
11
is.
Before software programs this field to 01b, it must also program the ALDWTC field to the desired value (a value of 0 is not
valid).
BIOS is required to program this field to ‘01’.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 103/242

SATA Backbone and Link Clocks Async Relationship Select (LGCLKASYNCSEL): This bit determines how SATA Controller shall
treat the relationship of bb_cclk and st_lgclk for its internal logic usage.
4 RW 0 “0” = bb_cclk and st_lgclk are treated as Synchronous with stepping-stone structure. This is the behaviour in previous SATA
Host Controller.
“1” = bb_cclk and st_lgclk are unconditionally treated as Asynchronous to each other.

SATA Ring Oscillator Clock Global Partition Clock Gating Policy (SOSCPCGP):
‘0’: SATA Ring Oscillator global partition clock gating logic to use SoC’s signal pmc_cs2 as the gate and ungate condition. This
means the clock gating entry can be delayed to sometime after SATA SIP local clock gating condition is true. Also the clock
5 RW 1 gating exit can happen before SATA SIP local clock gating condition is true.
‘1’: SATA Ring Oscillator global partition clock gating logic to NOT use SoC’s signal pmc_cs2 as the gate and ungate condition.
This means the clock gating entry happen when SATA SIP local clock gating condition is true instead of later. Also the clock
gating exit happen only when SATA SIP local clock gating condition is true instead of earlier.
Maximum Inflight Memory Data Read Size (MIMDRS[4:0]):
4:0 RW 0 Specify the maximum size of inflight data and ACMD read from memory, in the unit of 64B. 1-based. 0 means no maximum
limit, 1 means 64B, 2 means 128B and so on.

31:29 RW 0 Reserved
IP Inaccessible Dynamic Power Gating Policy for PIPE Interface (IIDPGPP): When this bit is cleared, SATA SIP will take into
account the PIPE interface idleness as the IP inaccessible dynamic power gating entry condition unless ResetPrep message
28 RW 0
had been received before the ForcePwrGatePOK message. When this bit is set, SATA SIP will not take into account the PIPE
interface idleness as the IP inaccessible dynamic power gating entry condition.
IP Inaccessible Dynamic Power Gating Policy (IIDPGP): When this bit is cleared, SATA SIP will commit to the IP inaccessible
dynamic power gating once Agent ISMs had entered the idle state disregard of the status of all other HW idleness. When this
27 RW 0 bit is set, SATA SIP will commit to the IP inaccessible dynamic power gating only when Agent ISMs had entered the idle state
and also all other HW idleness had achieved. “other HW” governed by this bits does not include the PIPE interface. PIPE
interface is governed by the policy bit IIDPGPP.

after hardware had achieved idleness and before the IP Inaccessible dynamic power gating flow or Warm Reset flow is

SATA IOSF Primary Clock Partition Clock Gating Hysteresis Timer (SIPPCGHT[1:0]):
‘00’: 16 clock (128 ns for prim_clk=125 MHz)
15:14 RW 0 ‘01’: 64 clocks (512 ns for prim_clk=125 MHz)
‘10’: 128 clocks (1.024 us for prim_clk=125 MHz)
‘11’: 256 clocks (2.048 us for prim_clk=125 MHz)
Slumber Hysteresis Timer Multiplier (SHTM): The multiplication result of this field and CTM5.PSHT field are used together to
indicate the Hysteresis delay before the Link Power Management state transition from Active into Slumber triggered by AHCI
Aggressive Link Power Management feature (PxCMD.ALPE, PTM2.P#ALPDLYE), or Partial to Slumber triggered by the AHCI
Automatic Partial Slumber transition feature (PxCMD.APSTE).
SHTM is decoded as below.
000 - Multiply by 1
001 - Multiply by 2
010 - Multiply by 4
13:11 RW 0
011 - Multiply by 8
100 - Multiply by 16
101 - Multiply by 32
110 - Multiply by 64
111 - Multiply by 128
With a free running common tick shared by all SATA ports, the actual Hysteresis delay may varies between +0 and +1 tick. For
example, when PSHT=20h and SHTM=11b, the Partial to Slumber Hysteresis could be 256 tick or 257 ticks, i.e. between
1.048576μs and 1.052672μs for the 125 MHz free running clock case.
Partial/Slumber Hysteresis Timer (PSHT): This field alone indicates the Hysteresis delay before the Link Power Management
state transition from Active into Partial triggered by AHCI Aggressive Link Power Management feature (PxCMD.ALPE,
PTM2.P#ALPDLYE). The multiplication result of this field and SHTM field are used together to indicate the Hysteresis delay
before the Link Power Management state transition from Active into Slumber triggered by AHCI Aggressive Link Power
Management feature (PxCMD.ALPE, PTM2.P#ALPDLYE), or Partial to Slumber triggered by the AHCI Automatic Partial
Slumber transition feature (PxCMD.APSTE).
PSHT is 1-based. It indicates the number of clock tick to wait prior to the Link Power Management state transition. The tick
varies according the sosc_clk frequency. The supported variations are shown in the table below:
sosc_clk Number of sosc_clk to Default Hysteresis, +0/+1 Programmable Hysteresis range,
Tick (us)
frequency form 1 tick tick (us) with +1 tick (us)
10:0 RW 20h 125 2^9 4.096 131.072 / 135.168 0 .. 8,388.608
120 2^9 4.267 136.533 / 140.800 0 .. 8,738.133
100 2^9 5.12 163.840 / 168.960 0 .. 10,485.760
25 2^7 5.12 163.840 / 168.960 0 .. 10,485.760
19.2 2^7 6.667 213.333 / 220.000 0 .. 13,653.333
With a sosc_clk common tick shared by all SATA logic, the actual Hysteresis delay may varies between +0 and +1 tick. For
example, when PSHT=04h, the Active to Partial Hysteresis could be 4 tick or 5 ticks, i.e. between 16.384μs and 20.48μs for the
125 MHz free running clock case.
Based on 125Mhz free running clock, 11-bit provides a range of 0 to 8.384512ms. (+1 clk tick = 8.388608ms). The default
value of PSHT=20h covers 131.072μs (32 ticks) to 135.168μs (33 ticks).

10.4.32 SIR Index B4h - SATA HBP Register (SHBP)
Table: SIR Index B4h (source)
Bits Type Reset Description
SATA HBP CRC (SHC[15:0]): This register holds the value of the CRC that is generated from SATA CRC logic. It is used in
31:16 RO 0 conjunction with the HBP mode. This CRC should be only meant to be read through a tester pattern after traffic activity has
subsided.
SATA HBP Port Select (SHPS[15:12]): These bits control which port will be activated under SATA HBP mode. These bits are only
valid when the SATA HBP Mode Enable bit is set.
Others: Reserved
0111b: SATA port 7
0110b: SATA port 6
15:12 RW 0 0101b: SATA Port 5
0100b: SATA Port 4
0011b: SATA Port 3
0010b: SATA Port 2
0001b: SATA Port 1
0000b: SATA Port 0
SATA HBP CRC Counter (SHCC): This register holds the value of the counter that is generated from SATA CRC logic. It is used in
conjunction with the HBP mode. This counter should be only meant to be read through a tester pattern after traffic activity has
11:2 RO 0
subsided. The counter is default to zero and increment by one for each DW put into the CRC logic. This 10-bit counter count from
zero to 1023 then roll over to zero. It’s reset to zero by SHCR.
SATA HBP CRC Reset (SHCR): When set by SW, this bit causes an internal reset to the HBP CRC logic and the 16-bit HBP CRC in
1 RW 0 SHBP.SHC[15:0] and SHBP.SHCC[9:0]. When cleared by SW, that internal reset is deasserted. After set it to one, SW supposed to
clear it back to 0 before starting the HBP operation.
SATA HBP Mode Enable (SHME): When this bit is set, the SATA HBP
0 RW 0 Mode is activated and the appropriate IO signals are activated.
When cleared, SATA HBP mode is disable, transmit and receive data goes through SATA AFE.

Port 0 OOB Retry Count Overflow
This bit is set if more than seven instances of COMRESET or COMINIT retry have occurred on port 0 since reset or since the last

3 RW0C 0

this value at the end of testing.
his register is only applicable to project(s) that have port 0 physically.
Port 0 OOB Retry Count
This count value indicates the number of times that a COMRESET or COMINIT retry has occurred on port 0 due to time out
conditions since reset or since the last time this bit was cleared. If bit 3 was set by hardware, then the value in this field is
2:0 RW0C 000 meaningless because more than 7 retries occurred. SW clears this bit by writing a ‘0’ to it.
There is no synchronizing logic on these bits, so to get a reliable result, SW should not read during a port reset or port power
state transition. Intended usage is to complete testing, then read this value at the end of testing.

OBFF Bus Master Coalesce (OBFFBMC): When set to ‘1’ and IOSF OBFF signaling indicates IDLE or “MEM CLOSED” pattern,
SATA host controller shall start coalescing all bus master traffic (i.e. DMA and FIS posting) on IOSF Primariry interface . If
RW / there is AHCI command outstanding when the coalescing signaling is received, the existing in flight data transfer shall be
5 0
RO completed up to AHCI command
This is meant for Server OBFF and only supported in AHCI/RAID mode.
If Server Feature (SATA OBFF) Disable Fuse is set to 1, this bit is held at a value of zero and is read-only, else it’s RW.
OBFF IRQ Masking (OBFFIRQM): When set to ‘1’ and IOSF OBFF signaling indicates non-“CPU ACTIVE” pattern, SATA host
RW / controller shall temporary mask interrupts (wire or MSI).
4 0
RO This is meant for Server OBFF.
If Server Feature (SATA OBFF) Disable Fuse is set to 1, this bit is held at a value of zero and is read-only, else it’s RW.
Slumber LTR Alternative Enable (LTRSLBEN): This bit affects the LTR reporting when all ports are in Slumber or equivalent
state.
‘1’: If PxCI !=0 or PxSACT!=0, and even if all ports in Slumber, Listen Mode or equivalent, do not allow SATA PLL shutdown and
3 RW/L 0 report Latency Tolerance equivalent to Partial.
‘0’ : When all ports are in Slumber or equivalent state, report LTR for Slumber.
This bit is not writeable when LTR_LOCK is set.
BIOS is required to program the field to ‘1’.
LTR Override Enable (LTR_OVR_EN):
While this bit is set to ‘1’, the device must bypass its normal LTR selection logic. See LTR_MSG_OVR for the expected behavior.
2 RW/L 0
If this bit is ‘0’ then LTR_MSG_OVR is ignored and the device’s normal LTR selection logic applies.
This bit is not writeable when LTR_LOCK is set.
LTR Lock (LTR_LOCK):
1 RW/L 0 Setting this bit to ‘1’ locks down all of the register fields associated with latency tolerance reporting from this device.
This bit is not writeable when LTR_LOCK is set (i.e. this bit is self-locking).
SATA LTR Mechanism Enable (SATALTREN):
0 RW 0
When Set to ‘1’, this bit enables the Latency Tolerance Reporting (LTR) mechanism.
10.4.37 SIR Index CCh - CPPM LTR Slumber (CLTRSLB)
Table: SIR Index CCh (source)
Bits Type Reset Description
Latency Requirement (LAT_REQ):
If this bit is set to ‘1’ then the latency tolerance is LAT_VAL multiplied by LAT_SCALE. This is applicable when all SATA ports are
15 RW/L 0b in Slumber or equivalent state with the exception when CLLM.LTRSLBDIS=‘0’.
Leaving this bit at ‘0’ implies no latency requirement (i.e. infinite latency).
This bit is not writeable when LTR_LOCK is set.
14:13 RO 00b Reserved
Latency Scale (LAT_SCALE):
Specifies the scale for the value reported in the LAT_VAL field.
Encodings:
000 - Value times 1 ns
001 - Value times 32 ns
12:10 RW/L 000b 010 - Value times 1,024 ns
011 - Value times 32,768 ns
100 - Value times 1,048,576 ns
101 - Value times 33,554,432 ns
110-111 - Not Permitted
This field is not writeable when LTR_LOCK is set. BIOS is required to program the field to ‘100’.
Latency Value (LAT_VAL):
The actual latency tolerance is this value multiplied by LAT_SCALE.
9:0 RW/L 000h
This field is not writeable when LTR_LOCK is set.
Slumber LTR value of 1ms is recommended. BIOS is required to program the field to ‘001h’.

10.4.40 SIR Index D2h - CPPM LTR Message Override (CLTRMSGOVR)
Table: SIR Index D2h (source)
Bits Type Reset Description
LTR Message Override (LTR_MSG_OVR):
While LTR_OVR_EN=1 and LTR_LOCK=0, every time this register is written to by software the device should transmit a new
internal LTR message whose data payload matches the contents of this register. See the IOSF spec for the format of the LTR
31:16 RW/L 0b
message.
Since SATA does not utilize the non-snoop portion, the upper DW of the 32-bit message is always 0’s.
This bit is not writeable when LTR_LOCK is set.

15.6 Security Audits
Not applicable to this IP
SATA IP is yet to complete SDL accessment. As an IP provider to multiple SoC that went through SDL process, SATA SIP is treated as a
low risk IP before integration – internal buffers are not exposed, no unique identifiers are supported, pre-Si validation is in place, etc.
15.7 Security Objectives and Assets
Prevent an attacker from upgrading premium SATA services from SW or BIOS.
15.8 Access Control Rules
Agents other than DMI, Fuse pull and DFX are restricted to access this IP.

Set ID request
Each message is compared with respective a SAI value, which is parameterizable by the SoC.
All other ingress messages are not SAI checked.
SAI Generation
Generated in IOSF Master interface for all upstream transactions with the same SAI value, which is parameterizable by the SoC.
Generated in IOSF Sideband interface for 4 egress messages only:
IP_Ready
Fuse pull request
Soft strap pull request
Set ID completion
The SAI values are parameterizable by the SoC.

15.10 Security Threats and Attack Points
SKU Disabled Features hacked to become enabled.
Features in SATA enabled through 2 mechanisms:
Fuses and soft straps
Register configurations
Functional Behavior changed to mal behaved
Malicious programming to Functional/DFx register in SATA which results in:
Denial of service
Unauthorized DMA Access from SATA SIP
SATA SIP does not prevent the programming of any address in the DMA engine(s) for it to operate on.It is up to the SoC and platform
teams to design how sensitive memory areas (DRAM, SRAM, etc.) are protected against unauthorized access by an unauthorized DMA
agent, for instance, by using SAIs.

Has the IP previously gone through the SDL process? - NO

Does the IP execute any software or contain a ROM? - NO

NOTE: SAI generate/checking on Sideband, SAI generate on Primary
Are the SAI access control policies reconfigurable? - NO
On the IOSF (Primary or Sideband), can the IP generate new transactions which are not related to a response? - NO

to GND, etc.? - NO

modified e.g. overriding SAI or “locking” protections, etc.? - NO
Is there any logic in the IP that can be used to bypass DFx protections? - NO
Is there logic in the IP that adds extra security protections to the DFx security encodings? - NO

15.12 Security S2 and Integration HAS
This Security S2 and Integration HAS is compliant to ” Security S2 and Integration HAS format towards parameter goldenization”
maintained by CEG IPAS (Sweta Barkur Sadashiva and Sue Ann Ung).
15.12.1 Introduction
List all the security related parameters for S2 review and for Integration HAS update.
This includes policy register default value parameters, security straps, hardcoded SAI parameters and security feature enable/disables.
This will enable the security team to make informed decisions on the list of trusted agents per IP and goldenize the values per project.
Along with this it enables the SOC to go over the integration, RDL/RAL generation, CRIF generation and security validation in a bug less
way.

24 Theory of Operation
24.1 Software Modes
There are two modes that software could be operating in: AHCI and RAID. RAID and AHCI modes require specific OS driver support and
are identical except for differences in GHC_PI and CC.SCC values.
24.1.1 Mode Switching
Software mode could change from one to another by manipulating the SATAGC.SMS register. There are some specific requirements for
the register:
SATAGC.SMS register must survive across D3 to D0 power state transitions.
hot
SATAGC.SMS shall not be manipulated during runtime operation; i.e. the OS will not do this. However, the BIOS may choose to change
it during POST to switch to other software mode.
The BIOS should also ensure that the function disable bits are set or cleared correctly.
24.1.2 AHCI Mode and RAID Mode
RAID and AHCI modes have the following requirements/characteristics:
RAID
Default CC.SCC = 04h
Default GHC_PI = 00h (read-only)
Has a unique Device ID
AHCI
Default CC.SCC = 06h
Default GHC_PI = 01h (read-only)
Has a unique Device ID
Both RAID and AHCI
BAR0and BAR1 are functional as Memory BARs for MSI-X.
BAR4 (i.e. AIDPBA) are functional as I/O BARs for AHCI Index Data Pair.
BAR5 (i.e. ABAR) is functional as the AHCI memory BAR.
MSI and MSI-X are supported.
SATAGC.SMS is 0b for AHCI mode and 1b for RAID mode.
BIOS should program SATAGC.SMS to the desired value. It is the responsibility of BIOS to re-configure SATAGC.SMS in order to change
to the targeted mode.
24.2 AHCI Operation
24.2.1 System Memory Structures
The serial ATA controller supports up to 8 ports, and each port supports 32 commands. The command list and received FIS may live in
64-bit space.
Refer to the AHCI specification for the system memory structures.
24.2.2 Data Transfer Operation
Refer to chapter 5 of the AHCI specification for details.
24.2.3 Error Reporting and Recovery
All errors within an HBA, occur within ports. There are no errors that apply to the entire host controller. There are several sources of
errors that could occur during a transfer. Examples of errors are:
System Memory - Bad system memory pointers cause data fetches and stores to be lost
Interface / Device - such as CRC problems, illegal state machine transitions, etc.
NOTE: This section is from the AHCI specification version 1.072, Chapter 6 with a few sentences added.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 131/242
=== PAGE 132 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
24.2.3.1 Error Types
24.2.3.1.1 System Memory Errors
System memory errors such as target abort, master abort, and parity may cause the host to stop processing the currently running
command. These are serious errors that cannot be recovered from without software intervention (see section Software Error Recovery).
A master/target abort error occurs when system software has given a pointer to the HBA that does not exist in physical memory. When
this occurs, the HBA aborts the transfer (if necessary) as described in . When this is complete, the HBA sets PxIS.HBFS. If PxIE.HBFE
and GHC.IE are set, the HBA shall also generate an interrupt.
A data error (such as CRC or parity), may or may not be transient. If the error occurred on a fetch of a CFIS, PRD entry or command list,
the HBA shall stop. If the error occurred on a data FIS or the ACMD field, the HBA is allowed to stop, but may also continue. When a data
error occurs, the HBA aborts the transfer (if necessary) as described in section HBA Aborting a Transfer. When this is complete, the
HBA sets PxIS.HBDS. If PxIE.HBDE and GHC.IE are set, the HBA shall also generate an interrupt.
If the HBA continue after a data error on a data or ACMD field, it shall poison the CRC of the Data FIS it transfers to the device.
24.2.3.1.2 Interface Errors
Interface errors are errors that occur due to electrical issues on the interface, or protocol miscommunication between the device and
HBA. Depending on the type of error, different bits in the PxSERR register are set. When these bits are set, either PxIS.IFS (fatal) or
PxIS.INFS (non-fatal) is set, and if enabled, the HBA generates an interrupt.
Conditions that cause PxIS.IFS/PxIS.INFS to be set are:
In the PxSERR.ERR field, the P bit is set to ‘1’
In the PxSERR.DIAG field, the C or H bit is set to ‘1’
PhyRdy drops unexpectedly
Examples of these types of errors are shown in Table: Error Examples, with the corresponding PxSERR bit that is set if appropriate.
The only difference between PxIS.IFS and PxIS.INFS being set is the type of FIS that is being transmitted/received when the error
occurs. If the error occurred during a non-Data FIS, the FIS must be retransmitted, so the error is non-fatal and PxIS.INFS is set. If the
error occurred during a Data FIS, the transfer shall stop, so the error is fatal and PxIS.IFS is set.
In the case of a non-Data FIS error, between seeing a non-Data FIS fail and the attempt to re-transmit, the HBA may receive other FISes
from the device (this will most likely happen when performing native command queuing commands). When this occurs, the HBA must
accept the FIS, perform the correct actions, and then retry the failed FIS.
If the HBA was transmitting a Data FIS it does not retry the FIS and waits for software to clear the PxCMD.ST bit to ‘0’. The HBA shall
retransmit a non-Data FIS continuously after a failure until either the transfer succeeds or system software stops the controller by
clearing PxCMD.ST to ‘0’.

the received FIS area based on the received FIS (i.e. the FIS is ignored). No error bits are set because this is a valid occurrence when
enumerating devices on a Port Multiplier.
It is the system software’s responsibility to check the PxSERR register periodically to determine if the interface is operating cleanly, and
take appropriate actions (such as going down to Generation1 speed if operating at a higher speed or notifying the user) when interface
errors occur.
Note that when an error such as a bad CRC or an illegal length occurs, the HBA cannot trust the incoming FIS to be correct. For
example, the HBA may have thought the FIS was a D2H Register FIS, but if the CRC is incorrect, it could be because the type specified in
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 132/242
=== PAGE 133 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
the FIS is incorrect.
To address this problem, the HBA shall not update its internal registers, nor update the FIS received area, until it determines that a non-
Data FIS it received was valid. If the HBA believes the FIS to be a Data FIS, however, it may copy it to memory at the appropriate PRD
location. This is because Data FISes may be as long as 8KB. An HBA may set other error or diagnostic bits based on the FIS contents
when there is a CRC error; these bits may not be completely accurate due to the CRC error. It is possible that a fatal error is generated
due to a non-fatal CRC error. In this case, software should perform the normal procedure to recover from a fatal error.
24.2.3.1.3 Port Multiplier Errors
When a Port Multiplier is connected, if a FIS is received from a device, where the PMP field does not match what is expected, the HBA
returns R_OK and sets PxIS.IPMS. The HBA shall discard the packet and not update any registers or memory structures based on the
FIS contents. In command-based switching, this indicates that the only active PMP port was not properly returned. In FIS based
switching, this indicates that a PMP field was returned that is not in the list of active PMPs.
24.2.3.1.4 Device Errors
When a FIS arrives that updates the taskfile, the HBA checks to see if PxTFD.STS.ERR is set. If it is, and PxIE.TFEE is set, the HBA shall
generate an interrupt and stop processing any more commands.
24.2.3.1.5 Command List Overflow
Command list overflow is defined as software building a command table that has fewer total bytes than the transaction given to the
device. On device writes, the HBA will run out of data, and on reads, there will be no room to put the data.
For an overflow on data read, either PIO or DMA, the HBA sets PxIS.OFS, and if enabled via PxIE.OFE and GHC.IE, generate an interrupt.
When this condition occurs on data reads, the HBA shall make a best effort to continue, however the HBA may not be able to recover
without software intervention.
Overflow is a serious error, thus software should perform a fatal error recovery procedure to ensure that the HBA is brought back to a
known condition before continuing.
For an overflow on writes, the HBA may transmit HOLDs to the device since it does not have any data to satisfy the request size; a
COMRESET is required by software to clean up from this serious error.
For an overflow on data writes with DMA, the HBA does not know there is more data until it receives the next DMA Activate.
When this occurs, it may optionally set PxIS.OFS and attempt to terminate the transfer. However, this is a fatal condition, and an HBA is
allowed to hang on the transfer. For PIO writes, the HBA receives the PIO Setup FIS and therefore knows the length, and therefore may
optionally set PxIS.OFS.
However, by not satisfying the length, the transfer ends in an error, and software must recover.
Therefore setting PxIS.OFS is optional for both DMA and PIO data write conditions.
Detecting overflow and setting PxIS.OFS on native command queuing commands is optional.
24.2.3.1.6 Command List Underflow
Command list underflow is defined as software building a command table that has more total bytes than the transaction given to the
device.
For data writes, both PIO and DMA, the device shall detect an error and end the transfer. These errors are most likely going to be fatal
errors that will cause the port to be restarted. For data reads, the HBA shall update its PRD byte count with the total number of bytes
received from the last FIS, and may be able to continue normally, but is not required to.
The HBA is not required to detect underflow conditions for native command queuing commands.
24.2.3.1.7 Native Command Queuing Tag Errors
The HBA checks incoming DMA Setup FISes to ensure that the PxSACT register bit for that slot is set. This is to prevent unintended
data corruption either in system memory or device. HBA will stop and unable to continue.
When in FIS-Based Switching Mode, the HBA further checks that the incoming DMA Setup FIS’s tag of a particular PMP matches the
PxSACT register bit for that tag-slot is set and that the PxSACT register bit belongs to the PMP. HBA will stop and unable to continue.
Existing error mechanisms involving Start bit clearing, such as host bus failure, or bad protocol, are used to recover from this case.
24.2.3.1.8 PIO Data Transfer Errors
In accordance with Serial ATA 1.0a, Data FISes prior to the final Data FIS must be an integral number of Dwords.
If the HBA receives an intermediate Data FIS transfer request that is not an integral number of Dwords, the HBA shall set PxSERR.ERR.P
to ‘1’, set PxIS.IFS to ‘1’ and stop running until software restarts the port.
The HBA shall ensure that the size of the Data FIS received during a PIO command matches the size in the Transfer Count field of the
preceding PIO Setup FIS.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 133/242
=== PAGE 134 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
If the Data FIS size does not match the Transfer Count field in the preceding PIO Setup, the HBA shall respond with R_ERR to the Data
FIS, set PxSERR.ERR.P to ‘1’, set PxIS.IFS to ‘1’, and then stop running until software restarts the port.
24.2.3.2 Error Recovery
24.2.3.2.1 HBA Aborting a Transfer
When the HBA detects an error that it cannot recover from, it may need to end the transfer on the SATA interface.
To do this, the HBA asserts SYNC Escape to stop the bad FIS, and when the device is quiescent, returns to idle. The SATA device should
send a D2H Register FIS at this point, with the ERR bit set to indicate an error in the transfer.
When aborting a transfer, the HBA does not wait for the D2H Register FIS before proceeding with error recovery (such as setting
interrupt status bits and generating interrupts). This is because a device may be in a hung condition and cannot generate the D2H
Register FIS.
24.2.3.2.2 Software Error Recovery
When an interrupt is generated due to an error condition, software will attempt to recover.
Fatal errors (signified by the setting of PxIS.HBFS, PxIS.HBDS, PxIS.IFS, or PxIS.TFES) will cause the HBA to enter the ERR:Fatal state,
and clear PxCMD.CR. In this state, the HBA shall not issue any new commands nor acknowledge DMA Setup FISes to process any
native command queuing commands. To recover, the port must be restarted; the port is restarted by clearing PxCMD.ST to ‘0’ and then
setting PxCMD.ST to ‘1’.
For non-fatal errors (signified by the setting of PxIS.INFS or PxIS.OFS) the HBA continues to operate.
If the transfer was aborted (see section HBA Aborting a Transfer), the device is expected to send a D2H Register FIS with
PxTFD.STS.ERR set to ‘1’ and both PxTFD.STS.BSY and PxTFD.STS.DRQ cleared to ‘0’. Under this scenario, system software knows that
the device is in a stable state and transfers may be restarted without issuing a COMRESET to the device.
For fatal errors, software must determine which commands were not processed and either re-issue them or notify higher level software
that the command failed.
These procedures are included in the following sections.
To detect an error that requires software recovery actions to be performed, software should check whether any of the following status
bits are set on an interrupt: PxIS.HBFS, PxIS.HBDS, PxIS.IFS, and PxIS.TFES.
If any of these bits are set, software should perform the appropriate error recovery actions based on whether non-queued commands
were being issued or native command queuing commands were being issued.
24.2.3.2.3 Non-Queued Error Recovery
The flow for system software to recover from an error when non-queued commands are issued is as follows:
Reads PxCI to see which commands are still outstanding
Reads PxCMD.CCS to determine the slot that the HBA was processing when the error occurred
Clears PxCMD.ST to ‘0’ to reset the PxCI register, waits for PxCMD.CR to clear to ‘0’
Clears any error bits in PxSERR to enable capturing new errors.
Clears status bits in PxIS as appropriate
If PxTFD.STS.BSY or PxTFD.STS.DRQ is set to ‘1’, issue a COMRESET to the device to put it in an idle state
Sets PxCMD.ST to ‘1’ to enable issuing new commands
Optionally issue a command to gather information about the error, for example READ LOG EXT, if software did not have to perform a
reset (COMRESET or software reset) as part of the error recovery.
Software then either completes the command that had the error and commands still outstanding with error to higher level software, or
re-issues these commands to the device.
24.2.3.2.4 Native Command Queuing Error Recovery
The flow for system software to recover from an error when native command queuing commands are issued is as follows:
Reads PxSACT to see which commands have not yet completed
Clears PxCMD.ST to ‘0’ to reset the PxCI and PxSACT registers, waits for PxCMD.CR to clear to ‘0’
Clears any error bits in PxSERR to enable capturing new errors.
Clears status bits in PxIS as appropriate
If PxTFD.STS.BSY or PxTFD.STS.DRQ is set to ‘1’, issue a COMRESET to the device to put it in an idle state
Sets PxCMD.ST to ‘1’ to enable issuing new commands
Issue READ LOG EXT to determine the cause of the error condition if software did not have to perform a reset (COMRESET or
software reset) as part of the error recovery
Software then either completes commands that did not finish with error to higher level software, or re-issues them to the device.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 134/242
=== PAGE 135 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
24.2.3.2.5 Recovery of Unsolicited COMINIT
An unsolicited COMINIT is a COMINIT that is not received as a consequence of issuing a COMRESET to the device (refer to Serial ATA II:
Extensions to Serial ATA 1.0a revision 1.1). If the HBA receives an unsolicited COMINIT during normal operation, the HBA shall perform
the following actions:
Respond to the device with a COMRESET
Halt execution until PxIS.PCS is cleared to ‘0’ by software
To detect this condition, software should check whether PxIS.PCS is set to ‘1’ on an interrupt. The HBA cannot guarantee that a device
received a COMRESET because a COMINIT may appear to be solicited to the HBA if it happens to occur closely to an issued
COMRESET. Therefore, when software detects that PxIS.PCS is set, software should first issue a COMRESET to ensure that the device
receives a COMRESET. Then software should perform the appropriate actions to clear PxIS.PCS to ‘0’.
To recover, software should perform error recovery actions for a fatal error condition (including restarting the controller). Then software
should perform a re-enumeration to check whether a new device has been inserted.
24.2.4 Hot Plug Operation
Refer to chapter 7 of the AHCI specification for details. If GHC_CAP.SIS is set, the SATA controller uses the SATAGP as interlock
switches.
Hot Plug Surprise Removal Notification (without an Device Mechanical Presence mechanism) mode that is mutually exclusive with the
PARTIAL and SLUMBER power management states. The following conceptual flows describe the different software initialization steps
necessary to support Surprise Removal Notification or Power Management.
24.2.4.1 Per Port Software Initialization to Support Surprise Insertion and Removal Notification
1. Set PxSCTL.IPM to 3h to disable both PARTIAL and SLUMBER transitions invoked by the drive.
2. Set PxCMD.ALPE to 0 to disable aggressive power management by the HBA.
3. Set PxIE.PCE to 1 to enable interrupts for hot plug insertions.
4. The PxIS.PCS interrupt status bit indicates to software that a hot plug insertion event occurred.
5. Write a 1 to the PxSERR.DIAG.X bit to clear it and the PxIS.PCS bit.
After a drive is detected on the port
1. Set PxIE.PRCE to 1 to enable interrupts for hot plug removals.
2. Issue a Set Features command to the drive to disable interface power management.
3. The PxIS.PRCS interrupt status bit indicates to software that a hot plug removal event occurred.
4. After the drive is removed, set the PxIE.PRCE to 0 to disable interrupts so that only the PxIS.PCS bit will generate an insertion event
interrupt. If this bit is left enabled two interrupts may be generated on the insertion event.
24.2.4.2 Per Port Software Initialization to Support PARTIAL and SLUMBER Power
Management:
1. Set PxSCTL.IPM to 0h to enable PARTIAL and SLUMBER transitions for the HBA port.
2. Set PxCMD.ALPE and PxCMD.ASP appropriately based on aggressive power management policy.
3. Set PxIE.PRCE to 0 to disable interrupts when PhyRdy changes state due to PARTIAL and SLUMBER transitions
4. During active operation PxSSTS.DET = 3h indicates to software that the link is operational.
24.2.5 Power Management Operation
Refer to chapter 8 of the AHCI specification for details.
24.2.6 Platform Communication
24.2.6.1 Software Initialization of HBA
Refer to section 10.1 of the AHCI specification (http://developer.intel.com) for details of standard AHCI software initialization flow.
SATA Host Controller contains additional port enable bits which require BIOS to program prior to AHCI software initialization flow. Refer
to the configuration space register PCS.PxE register bits description.
24.2.6.2 Reset
Refer to section 10.4 of the AHCI specification (http://developer.intel.com) for details.
24.2.6.3 Interrupts
Refer to section 10.7 of the AHCI specification (http://developer.intel.com) for details.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 135/242

3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
24.2.6.4 Device Mechanical Presence Operation
Refer to section 10.8 of the AHCI specification (http://developer.intel.com) for details. This feature is enabled when platform BIOS maps
the SATAGP pins to the S-ATA controller, and it sets the GHC_CAP.SIS bit.
24.2.6.5 Cold Presence Detect Operation
The SATA host controller does not support cold presence detect.
24.2.6.6 Activity LED
The activity of various SATA ports (during normal operation) may be configured to be driven out on the SoC SATA_LEDB pin.
The pin is an active-low open-collector output. When SATA_LEDB is low, the LED is active. When SATA_LEDB is tri-stated, the LED is
inactive.
Refer to section 10.12 of the AHCI specification (http://developer.intel.com) for details of LED activity during normal operation.
24.2.6.7 BIST
Refer to section 10.13 of the AHCI specification (http://developer.intel.com) for details.
24.3 Additional Information
24.3.1 Staggered Spin-up Support
Staggered spin up feature enables a controller to individually spin up attached devices and is useful to avoid having a power supply that
must handle maximum current draw from all devices at the same time when applying power to the devices. In order for a system to
support staggered spin up, the devices, the host bus adapter (HBA) and BIOS/driver must all support staggered spin up.
Staggered spin-up is only performed when power is applied to the drive. If the drive has been spun-down due to an ATA command
(e.g. STANDBY) and the device continues to be powered, the drive will not spin-up again until access to the media is required - the
staggered spin-up mechanism is not invoked in this case. Note that when a system transitions to system power management state S3
or greater, the drive will cease having power applied. Since the drive loses power, when the drive is powered back on in the transition
back to S0, the drive will undergo another staggered spin-up process.
Staggered spin-up is mainly controlled by software via a sequencing of COMRESET signaling. Software can control staggered spin-up
via PxCMD.SUD bit, in addition to the port enable bits of the PCS configuration registers.
To support the feature, SATA controller has the following characteristics:
SSS bit of AHCI CAP register shall be ‘1’
SUD bit of AHCI port specific CMD register shall be of read/write attribute.
When GHC_CAP.SSS is ‘0’, the PxCMD.SUD bit (and internal spin up drive signals) of each port is set to ‘1’ by the controller. Note that in
SATA SIP, GHC_CAP.SSS is ‘1’ by default after coming out from reset but BIOS could choose to clear the bit to ‘0’.
When GHC_CAP.SSS is ‘1’, the PxCMD.SUD bit of each port is read/write. A port can be enabled by setting the PCS.PxE bit of the port in
the configuration space to ‘1’. Software/BIOS can then choose which drive to spin up (or spin down) by writing a ‘1’ (or a ‘0’) to
PxCMD.SUD bit of the port where the drive is attached to. This allows the software/BIOS to manipulate the internal per port spin up
drive signal of the controller indirectly. Note that PCS.PxE bit overrides the setting of PxCMD.SUD bit, e.g. if PCS.PxE is ‘0’, a drive
cannot be spun up.
To summarise, software does not need to perform any action except for enabling ports to spin up drives if GHC_CAP.SSS is ‘0’. If
GHC_CAP.SSS is ‘1’, software is responsible to spin up each drive.
The SATA controller behaves in the manner described in section 10.9 of the AHCI specification for more details on AHCI staggered spin
up.
Table: SATA Controller Characteristics
**PCS.PxE GHC_CAP.SSS PxCMD.SUD Result**
0 X X No COMRESET and no spin up.
1 0 0 Illegal. When GHC_CAP.SSS is ‘0’, PxCMD.SUD must be ‘1’.
1 0 1 COMRESET and spin up.
1 1 0 No COMRESET and no spin up.
1 1 1 COMRESET and spin up.

To properly perform staggered spin-up, platform BIOS/software must perform a sequence of events to ensure that a device is not
powered until requested.
24.3.1.1 Performing staggered spin-up when the SATA HBA is configured for AHCI or RAID
mode
Software/BIOS has to complete the following:
1. Program ABAR
2. Set AHCI GHC.AE, indicating AHCI mode of operation
3. Set AHCI GHC_CAP.SSS, to indicate support for staggered spin up.
While the SATA SIP defaults this value to ‘1’ on reset, SW shall set this bit to ‘1’ in order to make it read-only.
4. Set PCS.PxE bits. Since GHC.AE is set and PxCMD.SUD are ‘0’, this will not cause COMRESET.
5. Cycle through AHCI PxCMD.SUD bits. COMRESET shall be sent for ports whose PxCMD.SUD bit is written to ‘1’.
To disable AHCI staggered spin up support, right after system power up, platform BIOS must clear AHCI GHC_CAP.SSS to indicate no
support for staggered spin up. In this case, software only needs to set PCS.PxE bits to trigger COMRESET.
24.3.2 Message Signaled Interrupt and Message Signaled Interrupt-X
This controller support single MSI and MSI-X.
24.3.2.1 Interaction Between INTx# and MSI/MSI-X
Per the PCI spec, A function is permitted to implement both MSI and MSI-X, but system software is prohibited from enabling both at the
same time. If system software enables both at the same time, the result is undefined.
24.3.2.2 Interaction Between INTx# and Single MSI/MSI-X
This is the mode of interrupt operation if any of the following conditions are met:
Pin based interrupts are being used - MSI and MSI-X are disabled (MSICAP.MC.MSIE=‘0’ and MSIXCAP.MXC.MXE=‘0’)
Single MSI or single MSI-X is being used - MSI is enabled (MSICAP.MC.MSIE=‘1’) and MSICAP.MC.MME=0h, or, MSI-X is enabled
(MSIXCAP.MXC.MXE=‘1’) and MSIXCAP.MXC.TS=0h
Note that system software never enable both MSI and MSI-X simultaneously else the behavior is undefined.
When MSI is enabled (MMC.MSIE = 1) or MSI-X is enabled (MXC.MXE = 1), INTx# interrupt is disabled regardless of the setting of the
PCI CMD.ID bit. The state of the CMD.ID bit does not affect MSI and MSI-X generation. This is shown in Table: Interaction Between
INTx# and MSI/MSI-X.
Table: Interaction Between INTx# and MSI/MSI-X
**CMD.ID MMC.MSIE MXC.MXE INTx# Assertion MSI/MSI-XGeneration**
0 0 Yes No
0 1 No Yes
1 0 No No
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 137/242
=== PAGE 138 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
1 1 No Yes
When MSI is enabled (MMC.MSIE = 1) or MSI-X is enabled (MXC.MXE = 1), the IS.IPS[6:0] bits and all of the status bits in the PxIS
registers still get set when an MSI is generated just as they would for INTx# interrupts.
The PCI STS.IS bit will not reflect MSI interrupts.
24.3.2.3 Multiple MSI-X
SATA Host Controller support multiple MSI-X messages for better performance. It doesn’t support Multiple MSI. In Multiple MSI-X
mode, multiple interrupt messages are allocated for the controller; each port has its own interrupt message. To support Multiple MSI-X,
the MSIXCAP.MXC.TS field represents a zero-based value of the number of implemented ports in the global memory space GHC_PI
register. For example, if 3 ports are implemented, then the MSIXCAP.MXC.TS field must be 2h (3 interrupts).
Multiple-message MSI-X enables each port to send its own interrupt message, as opposed to a single message for all ports described
in section Interaction Between INTx# and Single MSI/MSI-X. When enabled for multiple message generation, generation of interrupts is
no longer controlled through the IS register. Hardware shall continue to set the IS register just as in the single message case, however
the IS register is not used to determine whether to generate an MSI-X. The GHC.IE register shall still be a global interrupt enable/disable
for all ports when multiple-message MSI-X is used.
Each port senss its own interrupt message. The mapping of ports to interrupts is done in a 1-1 relationship, for example, port 0 to MSI-X
table entry 0, port 1 to MSI-X table entry 1, and so on.
When generating an MSI-X message, a port looks at its PxIS register, and uses the following rules to generate a message:
If a new bit is set in PxIS, and the corresponding bit in PxIE is set, send a message
If bits are cleared in PxIS, and other bits remain set, if their corresponding bits in PxIE are set, send a message.
If a PxIE bit transitions from ‘0’ to ‘1’ and the corresponding bit in PxIS is set, send a message.
24.3.2.4 Multiple MSI-X When Fewer Messages Allocated Than Requested
For MSI-X based message, if hardware advertises multiple MSI-X (i.e. MSIXCAP.MXC.TS) then each port has its dedicated MSI-X vector.
Port 0 uses MSI-X table entry 0, Port 1 uses MSI-X table entry 1, and so on. If system software allocates fewer vectors, it uses aliasing
to handle this situation. For example, hardware supports three ports, each with an associated MSI-X table entry, but only two vectors are
allocated. System software could assign the two vectors {A, B} to the three entries as ABB or AAB.
24.3.2.5 MSI-X Pending Bit Operation
If mask=1, then block the MSI sending. Instead, set the pending bit.
If mask=1 cause the pending bit to be set, and later an unmask happen, then send the MSI. After send, clear the pending bit.
If mask=1 cause the pending bit to be set, and somehow the interrupt source is cleared, then clear the pending bit and no need to
send the MSI.
If mask=1 cause the pending bit to be set, and somehow the interrupt source is cleared, then clear the pending bit and no need to
send the MSI. But if later an interrupt source is set again then set the pending bit again if mask still =1.
A function must set and clear its pending bit as necessary to support “pure polling” mode of operation.
24.3.3 Asynchronous Notification
When a Set Device Bits FIS is received by the host controller and the “N” (Notification) bit is set, the SATA host controller sets the
PxIS.SDBS bit to “1” if the “I” bit is set in the Set Device Bits FIS. This will cause an interrupt to be generated if that interrupt is enabled.
SATA SIP supports command-based switching through a Port Multiplier. FIS-based switching is supported through a Port Multiplier. To
support asynchronous notification from any port behind a Port Multiplier, the bit in the SNotification register will be set and an interrupt
will be generated (if enabled) even when the PM Port field of a Set Device Bits FIS does not match the port currently being accessed.
Table: HBA Actions–PMP Valid/Invalid shows the HBA actions for the various cases of PMP valid/invalid and N-bit clear/set.

24.3.4 AHCI Index/Data Pair
Index-Data Pair (IDP) provides host software with a mechanism whereby registers implemented in the AHCI’s memory mapped I/O
space (> 1MB) can be read from or written to. On PC based platforms, host software (BIOS, Option ROMs, OSs) written to operate in
‘real-mode’ (8086 mode) is unable to access registers in a PCI function’s address space, if the address space is 1) memory mapped and
2) mapped above 1MB. This is problematic for host real-mode software running on an AHCI enabled system when:
An AHCI implementation does not support legacy access methods
An AHCI implementation supports legacy access mechanism and also supports > 4 devices (natively or via Port Multiplier)
The IDP mechanism allows host software to access all of the MMIO AHCI registers using indirect I/O addressing in lieu of direct
memory mapped I/O (MMIO) access via the AHCI Base Address Register (ABAR). A conceptual diagram of the AHCI IDP is shown in
Figure: Conceptual Diagram of the AHCI Index/Data Pair.
Figure: Conceptual Diagram of the AHCI Index/Data Pair
24.3.4.1 Rules/Restrictions
While the IDP provides host software with full access to the AHCI’s MMIO registers, there are a few rules and restrictions that must be
followed by both the hardware and host software implementations:
When supported, the use of the Index-Data pair by the host software is not gated by setting GHC.AE (AHCI Enable); the Index-Data
pair is functional, regardless of the setting of the AHCI Enable register and allows the host software full access to the AHCI HBA’s
MMIO register space. However, the host software is not permitted to initiate transactions to device(s) attached to any of the SATA
ports implemented by the AHCI HBA, prior to setting GHC.AE to ‘1’. An attempt to access devices attached to the AHCI HBA prior to
setting GHC.AE to ‘1’ will result in indeterminate behavior.
The Index-Data pair mechanism is intended for use by the host software that does not support AHCI through the memory mapped
register space. While it is not prohibited for the host software (that provides native AHCI support - e.g. OS device driver) to use IDP,
for performance and flexibility reasons, it is strongly recommended that such software not use IDP; rather the host software should
be designed to use AHCI in a completely native fashion (via ABAR). Host software should not alternate between the IDP and MMIO
access mechanism, as this may result in indeterminate behavior.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 139/242
=== PAGE 140 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller

The data register is a DWORD (4 bytes) in length. It is the reading or writing of this register by the host software that results in action by

24.3.4.4 Access Mechanism
Refer to section 10.14.4 of Serial ATA AHCI 1.3 specification (http://developer.intel.com).
24.3.4.5 Host Software Discoverability
Determining whether or not the AHCI HBA supports IDP can be accomplished through an examination of the AHCI’s PCI configuration
space. Specifically,the host software can determine the existence of a device capability list by examining bit 4 of the PCI Status register.
If this bit is ‘1’, then the AHCI HBA host software will examine byte zero of DWORD 13 in the AHCI HBA PCI configuration space -and this
byte identifies the capability of the first device in the capability pointer linked list. If this byte returns 12h when read, then the SATA
capability structure has been located. Otherwise, the host software should continue walking through the linked list until the SATA
capability id or the end of the linked list has been discovered. Details regarding PCI Capability pointers can be found in the PCI
Specification v2.3 (www.pcisig.org). Details regarding the exact structure of SATA capability registers can also be found in section
Offset ACh: SATACR1 - Serial ATA Capability Register 1.
24.3.5 Serial ATA Index/Data Pair
The SATA Host Controller does not support Serial ATA Index/Data Pair.

24.3.6.2 Clock Gating
The SATA Host Controller supports clock gating to save its clock tree power dynamically at various levels such as the clock tree at the
fub level (DCG), unit level (DCG), power domain level (PGDCG), partition level (PCG) and SoC level (TCG). Each level of clock gating has
its associated clock gating cell. For DCG, PGDCG and PCG, the clock gating cell is inserted inside the SATA Host Controller logic. For
TCG, it is housed in the SoC logic where the SATA Host Controller use the IOSF Spec-defined clkreq-clkack full handshake to inform the
SoC logic if it allows the clock to be gated.
The default value of clkreq-clkack depends on the dynamic power gating feature of the controller. If the dynamic power gating feature is
not supported, clkreq-clkack defaults to ungated. If the dynamic power gating feature is supported, and defaults to “power gate,” then
clkreq-clkack defaults to gated.
Table: SATA Power Management Summary Table 1 lists all clocks’ clock gating entry and exit conditions at various levels and the
corresponding BIOS policy bit. Below is a list of the general behavior of clock gating in the SATA Host Controller.
Clock gating is fully hardware autonomous without software intervention, once the BIOS enables the clock gating during boot.
A Fub level (DCG) clock gating condition is promoted to unit level (DCG) clock gating, which then promoted it to the power gating
domain level (PGDCG) and the partition level (PCG) and finally the SoC level (TCG).
In general, DCG levels are able to ungate within the same clock as the synchronous ungate event occurs. An exception is the pclk DCG,
which takes several clock cycles, as the ungating is done asynchronously. PGDCG, PCG and TCG take several clock cycles, as the
ungating is done asynchronously.
24.3.6.3 Automatic Transition of Partial-to_Slumber (APS)
APS enables the SATA host controller port to perform automatic transitions from Partial direct to Slumber state on a port-by-port basis.
This feature increases the Slumber state residency duration by enabling earlier entry. Figure: Power Profile with APS illustrates the
power profile differences with the APS feature.
Software should not enable this feature if software sets PxSCTL.IPM to 2h or 3h. We recommend that the BIOS and/or an AHCI device
driver enable Aggressive Link Power Management (PxCMD.ALPE=‘1’) in order to maximize the SATA host controller power savings.
A new capability bit “allowing Host with extended Partial exit latency” will be introduced in the Identify Device. Host software needs to
check during the device discovery process (Identify Device) if the capability bit is set. If a HDD indicates this capability, then the host
software enables the feature by setting PxCMD.APSTE to correspond to the port.
Per AHCI rev1.3 section 5.3.4.5: If an Automatic Partial to Slumber Transition occurs, PxSSTS.IPM will reflect that the host has entered
Slumber (PxSSTS.IPM = ‘6h’).
(Not for EDS: Performance concern associated with exit latency of Slumber is being addressed. Althought max of 10ms exit latency is
stated in SATA specification, SATA SIP’s exit latency has improved significantly down to in the order of 20 micro-sec. The bulk of the exit
latency from Slumber is used to bring up the SATA PLL.)
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 146/242

24.3.6.4 DEVSLP
Refer to SATA and AHCI specifications for the DEVSLP protocol, timing definition and software programming.
The SATA host controller supports a DEVSLP transition from Slumber state, on all ports. MOD-PHY supports Common Mode off or on
during Slumber. As a result, the SATA port may keep Common Mode on or off during the DEVSLP state.
The following applies to both software initiated (ICC) DEVSLP and Aggressive DEVSLP Management mechanisms:
The SATA host controller always disables the DEVSLP pin’s input sensing circuitry. This is so that the solution is compatible with the
device hot-plug, device fully power-gated (AOAC), or an external pull-up voltage that is lower than the DEVSLP circuit buffer voltage.
GHC_CAP2.DESO will be ‘1’. The SATA host controller only asserts DEVSLP when:
There is no AHCI command outstanding (refer to SATA and AHCI specs for details)
After DITO time out, in the case of Aggressive DEVSLP Management, and
The port interface is in Slumber state.
This might overlap with the MOD-PHY dynamic power gating flow
The per port DEVSLP Idle Time out (DITO) value needs to take into account the device’s DEVSLP exit latency (tDSW parameter). This
requires a software adjustment based on the device tDSW parameter, on a per port basis. During run time, depending on the apps that
are running, the RST architecture may re-apply its caching knowledge of current apps running in order to determine the best method to
adjust the PITO.
24.3.6.5 SATA MOD-PHY Power State
The SATA host controller is PIPE 4.1 compliant with the following characteristics:
PCLK is an output from individual SATA host controller port into the Physical Layer.
A 10-bit or 20-bit data path is used for both TX and RX (no 8-bit/16-bit support).
OOB transmission is achieved by the SATA host controller bit-stuffing the TX data path as well as toggling the PIPE TxElecIdle signal
corresponding to the (un)squelch duration.
It has a Nominal Empty Buffer mode.
Table: MOD-PHY Power State shows the MOD-PHY behavior in various Power State associated with SATA Host Controller Link state.

24.3.6.6 SATA MOD-PHY Dynamic Power Gating
SATA host controller MOD-PHY dynamic power gating at per port granularity is supported.
The criteria to initiate entry flow at the SATA host controller to power gate a MOD-PHY data lane are:
The feature is enabled via SATA SIR.PTM5.PHYDPGEP# register
If Listen Mode support is required for hot plug, then SATA MOD-PHY power gating feature cannot be enabled. Modphy Squelch
Circuit, part of the MOD-PHY power gating domain, is required to detect COMINIT during Listen Mode.
This feature can be enabled for hot plug via mechanical presence switch (SATAGP pin)
No AHCI command outstanding, and
Port in Slumber or Offline/disabled state.
SATA host controller initiates the MOD-PHY power gating flow immediately upon meeting the criteria above by putting MOD-PHY into a
MOD-PHY-specific power gating allowed power state via PIPE4 protocol.
At the MOD-PHY-PIPE interface level, with the SATA MOD-PHY power gating feature enabled, SATA maps the Slumber state to PIPE PS4
or PS3, and stays in that state. The SATA port that is disabled/offline stays in the default PS2-reset state.
The exit flow happens due to host software-initiated SATA interface wake:
PxSCTRL.DET=offline to active
PxCMD.ICC=active
PxCMD.SUD=0 to 1
PxCI/PxSACT!=0
PCS.PxE=0 to 1
The SATA host controller initiates the MOD-PHY power gating exit flow immediately upon meeting the above criteria.
Upon detecting that the SATA PLL is on, the SATA port having a software wake event transitions to the eventual PS0 state. A Slumber
SATA port that does not have a software-initiated wake event stays in the PS4 or PS3 state and the MOD-PHY power gating state until
there is a wake event. The disabled/offline state remains in the default PS2-reset state with MOD-PHY power gated.
The criteria to initiate entry flow at the SATA host controller to power gate MOD-PHY Common Lane is
SATA PLL is OFF.
During the MOD-PHY power gated condition, the OOB detection is not capable of detecting any OOB. The following is part of the
SoC storage requirement: Device will not initiate wake when there is no command outstanding
If device somehow sends ComWake during an idle condition, the ComWake maybe ignored until host sw wakes up the SATA host to
execute a new AHCI cmd.
Device error reporting with COMINIT may experience a long delay before the host responds with ComReset.
24.3.6.7 SATA Host Controller Dynamic Power Gating
The SATA Host Controller supports dynamic power gating of the host controller core logic itself when the host controller is idle or when
there is a force power down request from the PMC. This section first describes the Hardware idleness-triggered power gating, and then
describes the PMC force power down.
Hardware idleness-triggered power gating can be fully hardware-autonomous or can be under software controlled. The SATA Host
Controller saves the most context, using the State Retention flop. This is to support the hardware-autonomous scenario, that is, to make
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 149/242
=== PAGE 150 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
the power down fully transparent to the software.
The criteria used to initiate Hardware-idleness triggered power gating are:
SATA Power Gating Domain Dynamic Power Gating feature is enabled, i.e. CTM5.SPDPGE=1,
and
One of the following is true:
Hardware Autonomous Dynamic Power Gating feature is enabled, i.e. CTM5.SHADPGE=1, and SATA Host Controller hardware
is idle,
or
Run-Time D3-Hot Dynamic Power Gating feature is enabled, i.e. CTM5.SRHDPGE=1, and config register PMCS.PS=3, and SATA
Host Controller hardware is idle
or
Run-Time D3-Cold Dynamic Power Gating feature is enabled, i.e. CTM5.SRCDPGE=1, and corresponding SATA RTD3-Cold bit in
PMC register is set, and SATA Host Controller hardware is idle
and
SATA Dynamic Power Gating hystheresis timer had expired. The timer is specified by CTM5.DPGHT and CTM5. IADPGHTM,
and
The following clock gating enable bits are all true:
SATA PGD clock Gating is enabled, i.e. CTM4.SPGDCGD=0, and
SATA per Port PCLK DCG is enabled, i.e. PTM4.S#PCLKDCGE=“1”, and
SATA per Port bb_cclk DCG is enabled, i.e. PTM4.S#DCGE=“1”, and
SATA SATA Ring Oscillator Clock Global Partition Clock Gating is enabled, i.e. CTM4.SOSCPCGD=“0”,
and
MOD-PHY squelch circuit turned off enabled, as achieved by any one of the following:
Ports’ Device Sleep Feature is enabled. (Refer to section DEVSLP for more detail.)
or
Ports’ Squelch Off during Slumber State Feature is enabled, i.e. PTM1.P#SQOFFIDLED=1 for all port # that exist,
or,
SATA MOD-PHY Dynamic Power Gating feature is enabled, i.e. PTM5.PHYDPGEP#=1.
The SATA Host Controller hardware is idle when all criteria below are met:
No command outstanding in both host controller and device, that is, it detects that software is idle
and
MOD-PHY is put into slumber state and PLL is shut down
and
MOD-PHY squelch circuit is turned off
and
All clocks used SATA Host Controller are allowed to be gated. NOTE: If Listen Mode support is required for hot plug, then the SATA
Host Controller power gating will not be triggered, even if the feature is enabled.
The SATA Host Controller triggers the power down handshake with PMC once the above criteria are met. As part of the handshake, it is
possible that a wake event is detected to abort the power down. If there is no abort, then PMC will shutdown the Power FET of the SATA
Host Controller power domain. If there is an abort, the SATA Host Controller triggers the power down, once the power down condition is
met again.
The SATA Host Controller hardware exits the power gating state when one of following is true:
There is software accessing the SATA Host Controller, as detected by the IOSF Primary Fabric ISM exiting the IDLE state
The is an access to the SATA Host Controller from the IOSF Sideband, as detected by the router ISM exiting the IDLE state
There is a wake event from a SATA mechanical switch event, as detected by the sideband SATA GP wire toggling
There is an access to the SATA Host Controller from the Cycle Router, as detected by the sideband Cycle Router access request
There is a wake event from PMC via the wire pmc_ip_wake
The SATA Host Controller also supports a PMC force power request. The PMC force power down flow is initiated by the PMC sending a
force power down message to the SATA Host Controller. Once it receives the request, the SATA Host Controller force itself into an idle
state, as if it had entered natural idleness. As a result, the power down flow, which is similar to the Hardware idleness-triggered power
gating flow, is followed, with two exeptions:
1. If the IOSF Primary Fabric and the IOSF Sideband Router are informed that the SATA Host Controller has been “unplugged” by
deasserting the prim_pok and side_pok signals;
2. If the context of the Host Controller, including those in the State Retention flop, are guaranteed to be reset.
There are two types of PMC force power requests:
1. A request to drop prim_pok and side_pok signals is followed by power down, which is used by the PMC to bring the platform into Sx.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 150/242
=== PAGE 151 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
2. A request to drop prim_pok and side_pok signals only without being followed by power down, is used by the PMC to reset the
platform momentarily.
Both types are supported by the SATA Host Controller.
24.3.6.7.1 State Retention Flop
Total SR-Flop in SATA Controller (8-port) is as below:
**Unit SR Flop Count**
PLP (8 ports) 936
PL 24
VRUNIT 2005
VSUNIT (8 ports) 4040
VBUNIT 162
Total è 7167
24.3.6.8 LTR Messaging
The SATA Host Controller reports latency tolerance to the PMC according to the aggregate activity of all SATA ports. (Refer to CPPM
registers in SIR space for more details.)
This section covers the definition and high level implementation of LTR messaging (Latency Tolerance Reporting). The actual format of
the LT message sent on the IOSF Sideband is shown in section Egress Messages. This section also talks about the relationship
between SATA PLL shutdown and LTR messaging, as well as the relationship between port-based PCLK DCG and LTR messaging.
The goal for LTR messaging in the SATA cluster is to deliver 3 types of LTR messages to the PMC via the IOSF Sideband, based on the
aggregated power state among all the ports. These 3 LTR message types are:
LTR_ACTIVE msg
LTR_PARTIAL msg
LTR_SLUMBER msg
There are 4 SATA Low Power Mode defines in the SATA spec:
Partial
Slumber
Active
Off
The order of power consumption from high to low is Active, Partial, Slumber then Off. (This order is also true of the associated latency
tolerance from low to high.)
In SATA LTR messaging, the Slumber mode can be modified, if CPPMSM.LTRSLBEN=1 and the command is outstanding, to become
Partial, to speed up the wake up time needed during Slumber, if SSD is connected. When this modification happens, the PxSSTS.IPM
still reports the Slumber state. Slumber can be modified to become Partial, as well, if CTM2.PLLSHUTDIS=1. The modified Partial and
Slumber are now called LTRPartial and LTRSlumber. LTRSlumber is further split into three sub states:
LTRSlumber.Disable
LTRSlumber.Listen
LTRSlumber.Slumber.
Effectively, the LTR message is now a function of all the ports’ power states of LTRPartial, LTRSlumber, Active, and Off, as well as some
register bits.
The following are some definitions of terminology used in this section:
Table: Terminology Definitions for LTR Messaging (source)
Term Definition
LTRActive A port is not in LTRPartial or LTRSlumber
A port is in any of the following states:
Partial
or
“Slumber AND (register CPPMSM.LTRSLBEN=1 AND the corresponding port has command outstanding)”
LTRPartial
or
“Slumber AND CTM2.PLLSHUTDIS=1”
or
“Listen and CTM2.PLLSHUTDIS=1”

For example, if in a 6-port case, 1 port is in LTRPartial, 1 port is LTRSlumber.Slumber, 1 port is LTRSlumber.Listen, 3 ports are
