LTRSlumber.Disable, then the aggregated power state is LTRPartial.
Figure: Port-level LTR FSM
Figure: Port-level LTR FSM above shows the port-level LTR FSM. Each port has one FSM of this kind to track its LTR state. All the port
level LTR FSM states are sent to the Cluster level LT FSM shown in Figure: Cluster-level LTR FSM. The following are behaviorial
descriptions based on this port level LTR FSM:
A general timing rule used during the port level LTR state transition is that when there is a transition from a high to lower power state,
the LTR message is sent at the latest possible time during that transition, and when there is a transition from a low to higher power
state, the LTR message is sent at the earliest possible time during that transition. For example, for the transition from
PORTLTR_ACTIVE to PORTLTR_PARTIAL, the message is sent only after the MOD-PHY has entered the low power state and the local
PCLK is gated, while for transition from PORTLTR_PARTIAL to PORTLTR_ACTIVE, the message is sent right after the local PCLK is
ungated but before MOD-PHY has exited from low power state.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 152/242
=== PAGE 153 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
The only path to leave port Disable is to go Active. Port-based DCG is ungated first.
The only path to leave port Listen is to go Active. Port-based DCG is ungated first.
It is possible to transition from Active to any other states.
Going to Listen from Partial will go through Active. Port-based DCG is ungated first.
Going to Listen from Slumber is direct. Port-based DCG stays gated.
Going to Listen from disabled will go through Active. Port-based DCG is ungated first.
Going to Disabled from Partial will go through Active. Port-based DCG is ungate first.
Going to Disabled from Slumber will go through Active. Port-based DCG is ungated first.
Going to Disabled from Listen will go through Active. Port-based DCG is ungated first.
Going from Partial to Slumber is caused by APS’s need to go through Active. Port-based DCG is ungated first.
Going from Partial to Slumber, if caused by outstanding CI/SACT clear, is direct. Port-based DCG remains gated.
Going from Slumber to Partial, if caused by CI/SACT set, is direct. Port-based DCG is remain gated.
The Cluster-level LTR FSM arc is the trigger to the VBunit to send the LTR message to the PMC. If the arc happens too frequently, the
intermediate LTR message may be dropped. The first arc and the last arc are always guaranteed to be sent.
Figure: Cluster-level LTR FSM
SATA PLL on/off is tightly couple with the cluster Level LTR FSM where:
The SATA PLL is off during LTR_SLUMBER state
The SATA PLL is on during LTR_PARTIAL or LTR_ACTIVE state
24.3.6.9 OBFF
This section talks about the SATA OBFF (Opportunistic Buffer Flush and Fill) handling. SATA OBFF logic performs the following actions
on receiving various IOSF OBFF[1:0] value (as defined in Table: IOSF 1.0 Spec Defined OBFF Signal, an except from IOSFspec 1.0):
If the IOSF OBFF[1:0] signal is IDLE or MEM_CLOSED, SATA will suppress next interrupts assertion, if any, and pause the DMA at next
command boundary. When DMA is paused, SATA will issue HIPM request to bring SATA interface into low power state, if device agrees.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 153/242
=== PAGE 154 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
If the IOSF OBFF[1:0] signal is OBFF, SATA will suppress next interrupts assertion, if any. However, DMA is not paused and hence current
or next commands, if any, will continue to access system memory.
If the IOSF OBFF[1:0] signal is ACTIVE, SATA will resume normal operation to remove the suppression of next interrupt assertion, if any,
and remove the pause of DMA hence allow next command, if any, to execute.
Table: IOSF 1.0 Spec Defined OBFF Signal (source)
OBFF PCIe OBFF
Requested Agent Behavior
[1:0] State
00 IDLE The CPU is in Cx with the path to memory closed. Agents should hold all opportunistic traffic and interrupts.
The CPU is not executing instructions (that is, the CPU is in Cx), but the path to memory is open. Agents should send all
01 OBFF
opportunistic bus master traffic but hold all opportunistic interrupts.
The CPU is in C0 with path to memory closed. (For example, the upstream PCI Express link is in L1.) Agents should hold all
MEM opportunistic traffic and interrupts.
10
CLOSED NOTE: Agents are requested to implement this state even though there is no corresponding state in the PCIe OBFF ECN. It
is product-dependent whether the Fabric will actually drive this state.
11 CPU ACTIVE The CPU is in C0 with the path to memory open. Agents should send all traffic and interrupts (opportunistic or demand).
24.3.7 Enclosure Management
Enclosure management is a mechanism by which the storage driver can monitor and control auxiliary services in a drive enclosure. This
feature is only valid in AHCI/RAID mode.
This controller supports multiple-activity LEDs that show per-drive status information. Typical LEDs per drive include device activity,
fault (to indicate a failure), and locate (to show an operator the bay to remove/insert a drive from/into).
The enclosure management protocol in used is SGPIO (refer to the SGPIO specification SFF 8485 revision 0.7
ftp://ftp.seagate.com/sff/SFF-8485.PDF ). The protocol is used between the host controller and an enclosure processor that
controls/monitors the sensors/LEDs within the enclosure. This controller only supports LED message transmission and has three
SGPIO protocol signals implemented:
SClock
SDataout
SLoad
The following information is extracted from the AHCI specification revision 1.072 section 12 but with an additional description for SATA
SIP.
24.3.7.1 Mechanism
The enclosure management for this controller involves sending messages that control LEDs in the enclosure. The messages for this
function are stored after the normal registers in the AHCI BAR, at an implementation-specific offset from the beginning of the AHCI
BAR, as specified by the EM_LOC global register (section 0, Not applicable to this IP).
Offset 1Ch: EM_LOC - Enclosure Management Location).
Software creates messages for transmission in the enclosure management message buffer. Software then writes a register to cause
hardware to transmit the message or take appropriate action, based on the message content. The software should only create
message types supported by the controller that are LED messages for SATA SIP. If the software creates other non-LED message types
(like SAF-TE or SES-2), the SGPIO interface might hang and the result would be indeterminate.
During reset, all SGPIO pins will be in a tri-state state. The interface will continue staying in tri-state state after reset until the first
transmission happens, when software will program the message buffer and set the transmit bit CTL.TM. The host will initiate the
transmission by driving the SCLOCK, and at the same time drive the SLOAD to ‘0,’ prior to the actual bit stream transmission. The host
will drive SLOAD low for at least 5 SCLOCK cycles, then only start the bit stream, by driving the SLOAD to high. SLOAD will be driven high
for 1 SCLOCK cycle followed by a vendor-specific pattern that defaults to “0000,” if the software has yet to program the value. A total of
24 bit streams from 8 ports (Port0, Port1, Port2, Port3, Port4, Port5, Port 6 and Port 7) of 3 bit per port LED messages will be
transmitted on the SDATAOUT0 pin after the SLOAD is driven high for 1 SCLOCK. For an 8-SATA port configuration, only 4 ports(Port 4,
Port 5, Port 6 and Port 7) of 12 bit total LED messages, follow by 12 bits of tri-state value, will be transmitted out on the SDATAOUT1
pin. For a 6-SATA port configuration, only 2 ports (Port 4 and Port 5) of a 6-bit total LED message followed by 18 bits of tri-state value
will be transmitted out on the SDATAOUT1 pin. For a 4-SATA port configuration, the SDATAOUT1 pin is not required, hence it can be
always be tri-state.
All the default LED message values will be high prior to being set by software ,except for the Activity LED message, that is configured to
be hardware-driven, which will be generated based on the activity from the respective port. All the LED message values will be driven to
‘1’ for the port that is unimplemented, as indicated in the Port Implemented register, regardless of the software-programmed value
through the message buffer.
There are two different ways of the resetting PCH SGPIO interface:
Asynchronous reset
Synchronous reset
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 154/242
=== PAGE 155 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
Asynchronous reset is caused by platform reset, and will cause the SGPIO interface to be tri-state asynchronously. Synchronous reset is
caused by setting the CTL.RESET bit or HBA reset, where Host Controller will complete the existing full bit-stream transmission then
only tri-state all the SGPIO pins. After reset–both synchronous and asynchronous reset–the SGPIO pin will stay in tri-state.
NOTE: PCH does not guarantee the target SGPIO device or controller to be reset. Software is responsible for keeping the PCH SGPIO
interface in tri-state for 2 second in order to cause a reset on the target of the SGPIO interface.
24.3.7.2 Message Format
Messages shall be constructed with a one Dword header that describes the message to be sent followed by the actual message
contents. The first Dword shall be constructed as follows:
Table: Message Format Descriptions (source)

the message type is not programmed as ‘LED’ for this controller, the controller will not take any action to update its LEDs.
For an LED message type, the message size always consists of 4 bytes.

24.3.8 Command Completion Interrupt Coalescing
Not applicable to this IP
24.3.9 Function Level Reset (FLR)
Each SATA controller implements the FLR functionality, an addendum to PCIe specification, as a vendor-specific PCI Capability Pointer
(09h).
When software writes ‘1’ to the FCTL.FLR bit, the internal FLReset within the function does the following:
Resets all layers of the SATA host ports with each controller, inclusive of the SATA Transport Layer, Link Layer and Physical Layer, and
Triggers the host to transmit COMRESET. COMRESET occurs unconditionally on software-enabled ports as a result of the FLReset .
There is no race condition in between the FLReset and COMRESET.
The following registers in the PCI function are reset by the FLR:
Most of the PCI configuration registers
Some of the AHCI registers
None of the SATA SIR space registers
It is software’s responsibility to reprogram the PCI header BAR registers. All BARs in SATA controllers are reset by FLR, and VMM is
responsible for re-programming them.
Refer to the register description section for details of actual register being affected.
24.3.10 Staggering Port Power Sequencing for Power Delivery Power
To prevent large current spikes (di/dt) on the power supply, the SATA interface employs a staggering power sequence for power-on/off,
as well as during Partial and Slumber. Not all the ports will be enabled at the same time, but will be staggered with a programmable
delay. Refer to config register CTM4.PPST for more detail. Therefore at any one time, there can only be 1 port enabled and drawing
electrical current.
The shut down sequence is also done where the port is powered down in a stagger manner.
If any software code toggles the config PCS.PxE in a back-to-back manner - all the bit(s) that were previously ‘1’ become ‘0’ and those
that were ‘0’ become ‘1,’ in two successive config write cycles. Due to the effect of Staggered Port Power Sequencing (including reset),
some ports may not get disabled in the process.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 156/242
=== PAGE 157 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
While it maybe a rare case for software to execute such a sequence, the following recommendations are provided. The
recommendations for software that intends to clear all PCS.PxE bits that were previously ‘1’ to ‘0’, is that the software will poll on each
PCS.PxP to be ‘0’ before writing ‘1’ to the corresponding PCS.PxE bit.
24.3.11 BIOS Programming Requirement Associated with AHCI Ports Implemented
Register
As defined in the AHCI spec, the AHCI Ports Implemented (PI) register is a memory-mapped IO register. The register is meant to be
BIOS programmable with an “RWO” attribute. Its purpose is to inform the AHCI driver how many AHCI SATA ports are available in a
particular system. The AHCI driver uses the GHC_PI register content to begin device discovery through the implemented port-based
AHCI m.m.i.o. register offsets.
With the cmd/data pipelining capability on the IOSF Primary interface, a fencing mechanism is required in order to ensure that all port-
based read/write cycles only reach the port-based IOSF agent after the AHCI GHC_PI register has been programmed. For the BIOS
specification, the following programming requirement is imposed on the AHCI GHC_PI register programming relative to all subsequent
AHCI port-based register offsets programming:
(Applicable to the GHC_PI register at section Offset 0Ch: GHC_PI - Ports Implemented) After the BIOS issues the initial write to the AHCI
Ports Implemented (PI) register (after any PLTRST#), the BIOS is required to issue two reads to the AHCI Ports Implemented (PI)
register.
Furthermore, if, for some reason, the BIOS accesses any of the port-specific AHCI address range before setting the corresponding
GHC_PI bit, the BIOS is required to read the GHC_PI register before the initial write to the GHC_PI register, to achieve a similar fencing
mechanism.
The two non-posted reads are to guarantee that
No accesses to any port-based register offsets take place until after they write to the GHC_PI register.
They have been consumed by the hardware destination.
The earliest posted write to any port-based register offsets are only issued by the BIOS after the second non-posted read.
24.3.12 SATA Thermal Throttling
For SATA, thermal throttling is supported in AHCI operations only. The thermal throttling cluster provides T-state information according
to chip thermal condition. There are 4 global SoC throttle states, T0 to T3; where T0 is no throttling and T3 is highest throttling.
Theory of Operation:
In the T0 state, no thermal throttling is performed by SATA.
In the T{1, 2, 3} state, SATA begins to throttle command dispatching and make attempts to put the port into the LPM state.
The success of entering the LPM state depends solely on the SATA device’s own LPM policy, which may or may not acknowledge
SATA SIP’s request to enter LPM. The resulting LPM state also depends on Interface Power Management Transitions Allowed
(PxSCTL.IPM), Agressive Link Power Management (PxCMD.ASP and PxCMD.ALPE=‘1’).
Regardless of whether entry into LPM is successful or not, the AHCI command dispatching based on PxCI is blocked for a period of
time called T_inactive. All commands outstanding in SATA device are allowed to complete. This T_inactive period varies according to
the T_state.
Once T_inactive expires, command is allowed to be dispatched again for a period of time called T_dispatch. This period of time SATA
port behaves like normal essentially and is also free to enter/exit LPM state per normal host/device operation. T_dispatch is always
taken from T_dispatch register.
A non-T0 T_state indication causes the follow thermal throttling cycles: T_inactive (T#) → T_dispatch → T_inactive (T#) → T_dispatch
→ … where each T# maybe associated with different T_state.
According to Thermal Throttling chapter, the delay from one T-state change to the next T-state change is a minimum of 1 second.
When a different non-T0 T-state indication is seen by SATA, the effect of the new T-state will take effect when T_dispatch expires and
new T_inactive is loaded.
24.3.13 IOSF Sideband
SATA host controller supports IOSF Sideband Channel for register accesses and communication of miscellaneous status information
with multiple units in SoC.
SATA configuration space, I/O space, and AHCI memory-mapped IO space are accessible from the IOSF Sideband as well as IOSF
Primary channels. The intention of register access from the IOSF Sideband Interface is for survivability and testability purposes. The
SATA host controller does not ensure ordering between traffic on IOSF Primary and IOSF Sideband.

24.3.16 Disabling SATA Host Controller (PCI Function Disable)
There are various approaches to save the SATA Host Controller power when the SATA Host Controller is function disabled. Two
approaches are under PMC control and one approach is under SATA Host Controller control. For PMC controls, the first approach is for
PMC to not assert the pmc_ip_wake signal to the SATA Host Controller during boot, hence the SATA Host Controller continues to stay in
the default IP Inaccessible power-gated state. The second approach is for PMC to send the ForcePwrGatePOK sideband message to
the SATA Host Controller after the initial boot.
(Refer to Static Power Gating section in the Chassis Power gating HAS for more detail about both PMC controlled approaches. For
SATA Host Controller control, refer to the next paragraph for more details.)
When disabling the SATA Controller, the System BIOS must follow the procedures outlined below.
1. BIOS shall disable all ports by programming SATA SIP PCI configuration space offset 94h bit [0:7] (PCS.PxE) to “00h”.
2. BIOS shall not implement any AHCI ports by programming SATA SIP ABAR MMIO space offset 0Ch bit [0:7] (PI) to “00h”.
3. BIOS shall disable the Memory Space Enable and IOS Space Enable by programming SATA SIP PCI configuration space offset 04h bit
[1:0] to “00”.
4. Set Sata Port Clock Disable bits by programming SATA SIP PCI configuration space offset 90h bit [7:0] to FFh.
5. Enable all the Sata SIP dynamic clock gating and dynamic power gating feature:
a. Configuration SIR space offset 9Ch bit [29] (CTM2.SIDECLKDCGEN, SATA IOSF Sideband Dynamic Clock Gating Enable) to “1”.
b. Configuration SIR space offset 9Ch bit [23] (CTM2.SPLDCGE, SATA PLunit/PLPunit Dynamic Clock Gating Enable) to “1”.
c. Configuration SIR space offset 8Ch bit [23:16] (PTM4.S#PCLKDCGE, SATA Port# PCLK Dynamic clock gating enable) to “1”.
d. Configuration SIR space offset A0h bit [15] (CTM3.SVRDCGE, SATA VRunit/VBunit Dynamic Clock Gating Enable) to “1”.
e. Configuration SIR space offset 8Ch bit [7:0] (PTM4.S#DCGE, SATA Port# Backbone Clock Dynamic clock gating enable) to “1”.
f. Configuration SIR space offset A4h bit [15] (CTM4.SOSCPCGD, SATA Ring Oscillator Clock Global Partition Clock Gating Disable) to
“0”.
g. Configuration SIR space offset A4h bit [14] (CTM4.SOSCDCGE, SATA Ring Oscillator Clock Common Block Dynamic Clock Gating
Enable) to “1”.
h. Configuration SIR space offset 84h bit [27:24] (PTM2.P#SOSCDCGE, SATA Port # Ring Oscillator Clock Dynamic Clock Gating Enable)
to “1”.
i. Configuration SIR space offset A4h bit [5] (CTM4.SOSCPCGP, SATA Ring Oscillator Clock Global Partition Clock Gating Policy) to “1”.
j. Configuration SIR space offset A4h bit [26] (CTM4.SSPGDCGD, SATA Power Gating Domain Clock Gating Disable) to “0”.
k. Configuration SIR space offset A4h bit [25] (CTM4.SISPCGD, SATA IOSF Sideband Clock Partition Clock Gating Disable) to “0”.
l. Configuration SIR space offset A4h bit [24] (CTM4.SIPPCGD, SATA IOSF Primary Clock Partition Clock Gating Disable) to “0”.
m. Configuration SIR space offset A8h bit [17] (CTM5.SHADPGE, SATA Hardware Autonomous Dynamic Power Gating Enable) to “1”.
n. Configuration SIR space offset A8h bit [16] (CTM5.SPDPGE, SATA PGD Dynamic Power Gating Enable) to “1”.
6. Put SATA Controller into D3 by programming PMCS.PS in SATA Host Controller PCI configuration space offset 74h bit [1:0] to ‘11’.
a. Note: This is needed to support ADP_S late PCR 1507861810. Needed for ADP_S to support OS Idle when SATA Controller is Function
Disabled. See HSD 1507683402 where PMC OS Idle is defined as “SATA D3 indicator = ‘1’ but this requirement is not met in the case of
SATA Controller being function disabled. To w/a that PMC issue, this programming to SATA Controller as part of the SATA function
Disable flow cause the SATA D3 indicator to PMC to assert even through SATA Controller is not really in D3 usage. PMC’s OS handling
suppose to check for SATA Controller’s st_pmc_pg_req_b=0 and LTR, but it doesn’t (due to late PCR and hence to simplify the PMC
implementation!). Hence the w/a is to force SATA D3 indicator to ‘1’.
b. Note: There is no side effect to PMC with this step, in the case of non OS Idle, as PMC ignore the D3 indicator from SATA in non OS Idle
case.
7. Disable SATA Host Controller PCI function by doing read-modify-write for setting SATAGC.SCFD in SATA Host Controller PCI
configuration space offset 9Ch bit [10] to ‘1’.

24.3.18 PSF Ordering Requirment (Not for EDS)
The PSF that SATA attached to, is required to serve as the interconnect fabric between each of the SATA IOSF primary agents (VRunit,
VSunit0, VSunit1 and so on) to SoC backbone in order to ensure ordering requirement. This is achieved by using non-buffered Fabric
ports to connect the VRunit and all VSunit and all these Fabric ports are connected to a single buffered Agent port or Fabric port.
IOSF does not specify any transaction ordering rules between channels with different IOSF Primary Queue ID’s (QID). However SATA
host controller has transaction ordering among the SATA IOSF Primary agents due to the global and per-port AHCI memory mapped
register set being implemented in the VRunit and per-port VSunits, respectively.
24.3.19 SATA Port Physical Layer Lane MUXing
In allowing flexible system configuration of a number of SATA ports or PCIe/USB3/SSIC lanes, the SATA host controller supports SATA
port MUXing with PCIe/USB3/SSIC Laneto each lane of the physical layer (MOD-PHY). The MUXing is specified via the per-port
fuse(SPS#), soft-strap(SPS#,SPSGP#) and GPIO pin(SATAPORTSELECT_SATAGP#) in the following descending priority.
The MOD-PHY lane is owned by SATA if the following is true, otherwise, the MOD-PHY lane is not owned by SATA.
1. Fuse SPS#= “00”, else
2. Fuse SPS#= “10”, AND soft-strap SPS# = “00”, else
3. Fuse SPS#= “10”, AND soft-strap SPS#= “11”, AND sof-strap SPSGP#=“0”, AND GPIO pin SATAPORTSELECT_SATAGP#=“1”, else
4. Fuse SPS#= “10”, AND soft-strap SPS#= “11”, AND sof-strap SPSGP#=“1”, AND GPIO pin SATAPORTSELECT_SATAGP#=“0”, else
Table: SATA/PCIe Lane Muxing Polarity for Various Connectors shows the GPIO pin polarity defined in SATA Spec for various kind of
connectors. For example, in SATA Express connector, GPIO pin polarity ‘0’ means SATA device while ‘1’ means PCIe device.
Table: SATA/PCIe Lane Muxing Polarity for Various Connectors
**Connector SATA PCIe**
SATA Express 0 1
M.2’s CONFIG_1 0 1
mSATA 1 0
Table: SATA/USB3 Lane Muxing Polarity for Various Connectors

24.3.20 Register Space Trusted SAI Check
Trusted SAI Check is a mechanism to differentiate between the list of trusted SAI vs untrusted SAI in terms of the 3 register space
(Config Space, MMIO space and IO Space) access from both Primary and Sideband. Trusted SAI are like SMM, BIOS, TAM etc.
Untrusted SAI are untrusted device etc. G0SAIPOLCTRL[63:0] and G0SAIPOLAC[63:0] are two SAI security policy register to govern the 3
register spaces access. The default value of these 2 register are parameterize-able (G0SAIPOLCTRL_DEFAULT[63:0] and
G0SAIPOLAC_DEFAULT[63:0]) to allow SoC to choose the default secure SAI value that is allow to change the security policy at later
later time in post silicon. G0SAIPOLCTRL govern the write access to G0SAIPOLAC register and G0SAIPOLCTRL itself. G0SAIPOLAC
provide both read and write access to the 3 register spaces but excluding the G0SAIPOLCTRL and G0SAIPOLAC registers in the config
space. This is to avoid double policy to the G0SAIPOLCTRL and G0SAIPOLAC registers. Each bit in the G0SAIPOLCTRL and
G0SAIPOLAC is associated with the 6-bit SAI Index which is mapped to a particular 8-bit SAI value or values as shown in Table: 8-bit SAI
Value mapping to 6-bit SAI Index and Figure: 8-bit SAI Value Mapping to 6-bit SAI Index.
A value ‘1’ in G0SAIPOLCTRL means that a Config Cycle with SAI value that match the mapped 8-bit SAI value or values has the write
access to the G0SAIPOLCTRL register and G0SAIPOLAC register. Inversely, a value ‘0’ means no write access. Read access to both
G0SAIPOLCTRL and G0SAIPOLAC are always available. A value ‘1’ in G0SAIPOLLAC means that a Cycle with SAI value that match the
mapped 8-bit SAI value or values has both the read and write access to the 3 register spaces excluding the G0SAIPOLCTRL and
G0SAIPOLAC register. Inversely, a value ‘0’ means no read and write access.
Table: 8-bit SAI Value mapping to 6-bit SAI Index (source)
In Decimal
Usage
SAI[7:0] INDEX[5:0] Count of SAI[7:0] Count of INDEX[5:0]
All the odd number. In 8 groups:
Group 1 has SAI[3:0]=1 → INDEX[5:0]=0.
CPU Group 2 has SAI[3:0]=3 → INDEX[5:0]=1. 0 - 7 128 8
……
Group 8 has SAI[3:0]=15 → INDEX[5:0]=7
Untrusted Device 0 63 1 1
Assigned to Devices Even numbers between 16 and 124 8 - 62 55 55
Reserved for Devices Even numbers between 126 and 254 63 65 1 (repeated)
Unused Even number between 2 and 14 63 7 1 (repeated)
Total 256 64 + 1 repeat + 1 repeat

24.4 Performance Expectation (Not for EDS)
This whole section is not meant for EDS.
This section contains clarification of existing hardware goals unless specified otherwise.
24.4.1 Data Streaming
Each SATA port is able to stream Data FIS at max bandwidth per Gen speed: 600MB/s @ Gen3, 300MBs/s @ Gen2 and 150MB/s @
Gen1.
Each SATA port has 1K of streaming buffer. Unless throttled by the SoC backbone, the SATA host controller does not transmit HOLD
primitive during data in (data from a SATA device to the SATA host). For data out, each SATA port is designed to cater to reasonable
system memory read completion latency, which is 1.52μs for upstream read request size of 64B, or 1.42μs for upstream read request
size of 128B. As a result, the SATA host does not throttle H2D Data FIS transmission except for:
Initial start up latency associated with each H2D Data FIS (that is, there may be some HOLDs transmitted after the H2D Data FIS
header and before the payload data stream returns from system memory)
System memory read completion that exceeds the design target of 1.52μs or 1.42μs.
Refer to section Performance Requirement on the requirement to Fabric to fulfill SATA data streaming.
24.4.2 Power
All link power management (LPM) related features of AHCI and SATA are supported by the SATA host.
A device-initiated LPM request in the middle of an AHCI command execution is also supported.
24.5 DFx (Not for EDS)
This whole section is not meant for EDS, unless specifically stated otherwise.

24.5.2 BIST Activate FIS
The BIST Activate FIS is defined in the SATA spec. Table: BIST Activate FIS is applicable to all SATA ports.

24.5.2.1 Far End Retimed Loopback Mode
The SATA host controller can enter far end retimed loopback mode via the BIST Activate FIS sent by a SATA device (or test source). This
involves loopback from the host’s RX[port N] to the host’s Tx [port N] pins. It requires appropriately inserted retiming ALIGN primitives in
the loopback data stream. Refer to the SATA specification for details.
24.5.3 Additional SATA Test Modes
Host-Initiated Loopback is supported in AHCI mode. Compared to functional AHCI programming, the AHCI Loopback programming has
the following limitations:
The need to use PxCMD.CLO to clear the internal BSY bit before setting the PxCMD.ST to ‘1’, as there is no power-up register FIS from
device to clear the BSY bit.
Only one CI bit can be set per each Loopback session
Only one PRD can be used in each CI
Only PRD size of 256B is supported
Only CL.CFL=0 is supported (don’t care by HW)
Need to set the CL.P bit for the CI, to allow PRD prefetch, without waiting for device FIS
Need to clear the PxCMD.ST bit after the loopback session done and set it again to start another session
Figure: SATA Single Burst Loopback Mode, Figure: SATA Continuous Loopback Mode, and Figure: SATA Internal BERT Near-End
Loopback Mode show more illustrative loop-back diagrams for Single Burst Loop-back. They show Port 0 having near-end loopback to
Port 1. The opposite direction is also supported.
Figure: SATA Single Burst Loopback Mode

Typically, the number of port is an even number.
Hence they can be grouped into port-pairs like port 0-1, port 2-3, port 4-5, and so on. When the number of ports is an odd number, says 3
port, the grouping can be port 0-1, and port 1-2. In general, port N can be grouped with port N-1 or port N+1.
Software needs to be careful when programming the LBC##.LAS and LBC##.LARPDSS to pair one port with another port, and never pair
one port with 2 ports. For example, LBC01.LAS and LBC01.LARPDSS = “11” to loopback data from port 1 to port 0, and LBC23.LAS and
LBC23.LARPDSS = “10” to loopback data from port 1 to port 2 violates this requirement.

25 Micro Architecture
25.1 SATA Controller Micro Architecture
This section provides further elaboration of the SATA controller micro architecture for the VRunit, VSAunit, and PL* unit. The VSAUNIT
implements the command/DMA, transport and link layer of the SATA specification, mentioned earlier. The VRunit is the router and
configuration block for the SATA interface. It is primarily responsible for claiming, forwarding and completing non-port-specific memory,
configuration and I/O transactions for the SATA cluster. The PLPUNIT contains the per port interfacing logic for providing part of the
PHY layer control. The PLUNIT contains common PHY module control logic, e.g. PLL related configuration control.
The VSAUNIT is divided into several partitions that correspond to the SATA interface specification, as illustrated by Figure: VSAUNIT
Data Path. They are summarized as follows:
vsaBMU - Per port IOSF interface logic
vsaDMA - Per port DMA logic
vsaT and vsaMUX - Per port transport and MUX layer logic
vsaL - Per port link layer logic
Figure: VSAUNIT Data Path
The following sub-sections provide an additional next level detail of the SATA per port controller.
VSABMU
IOSF interface staging and decoding logic
BMU SRAM to stream data to the IOSF fabricVSADMA
Fetched PRD information
Byte Count Update (BCU) information
LBA
Command List information
VSAT
Transport packet header generation and Received Transport packet header from link layer
CFIS Transmit and Receive FIFO
BIST FIS Transmission
Taskfile to support legacy mode
VSAM
SYNCFIFO SRAM used to either stream data to BMU or CFIS to Transport layer
VSAL
CRC computation and insertion for transmit path
CRC checking for receive path
Transmit link primitive
VRAM FIFO to buffer data between Link layer and MUX layer
FIFO to buffer data between Link layer and Phy layer

25.1.1 VRUNIT
The VRunit is the router and configuration block for the SATA interface. It is primarily responsible for claiming, forwarding and
completing non-port-specific memory, configuration and I/O transactions for the SATA cluster. The other features provided by the
VRunit are:
Legacy bus master/slave emulation
SATA Index/Data Pair (SIDP)
AHCI Index/Data Pair (AIDP)
Single Message Signaled Interrupt (MSI) - AHCI mode only
D3 Power Management
Housing configuration registers, non-port-specific memory registers, and some IO registers
Support of up to 6 AHCI/RAID ports with one PCI function
Function Level Reset (FLR)
Host Bus Adapter (HBA) Reset
PME reporting
Pin interrupts
New Device IDs implementation
Housing DFT configuration registers
Trapping for SMI generation
Enclosure Management
Supporting NVRAM External Memory Device/Write Back Cache (EMD/WBC)’s RHCI space via D31F2’s ABAR as SATA Port 7
EMD/WBC is renamed as NVMHCI#1 in IBX.
Supporting NVRAM’s Solid-State Device (SSD) as SATA Port 6 with AHCI/RAID capability.
SATA Reset
Backbone Clock Gating (BCG)
Supporting Doze Mode (DM)
Thermal Reporting

25.1.1.2 VRunit IOSF Interfacing
25.1.1.2.1 VRunit IOSF Write Cycle
A write cycle is defined as a write transaction to the SATA controller initiated by the CPU or by a peer device. The VR unit does not
support bursting for write.
1. The write cycle to VR starts when iosf_tdec is asserted. The IOSF fabric forwards the transaction (address and attributes) to VRBMU
on the same bb_cclk cycle.
2. The request header is forwarded down to VRBMU. VRBMU decodes the address, commands and other attributes, and if it is the
target of the transaction by, dec_hit will be asserted on the same clock and signaled back to the fabric on the next cycle.
3. Once the IOSF fabric determines to send this command to the VR unit, it will assert ‘cput’ signal. VR unit will use it to decode the
command and get ready for receiving data on the next cycle.
4. On the next cycle of ‘cput’ siganl, the dput is asserted to drive data.
5. For the non-posted cycles, a completion will be sent back to the initiator. Section VRunit IOSF Completion Cycle has a more detailed
description of the completion cycle handling by the VR unit.
For source decode or fabric decode scenarios, skip step 1 and 2 (above). Figure: IOSF Request with Target Decode shows the IOSF
request timing diagram.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 170/242
=== PAGE 171 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
Figure: IOSF Request with Target Decode
Copied from Figure 2-16 of the IOSF Specification, version 0.82.
25.1.1.2.2 VRunit IOSF Read Cycle
A read cycle is defined as a read transaction to the SATA controller initiated by the CPU or by a peer device. Read cycles can be burst.
The max burst size is limited by the AHCI spec to 1 QW. The read cycle to VR starts when iosf_tdec is asserted similarly to a backbone
write cycle. The only difference is no ‘dput’ signal will be asserted for the read cycle.
25.1.1.2.3 VRunit IOSF Completion Cycle
A completion cycle is returned for all backbone read and write cycles except memory write (posted) cycles. A completion transaction
starts with VRBMU asserting req_avail to BBI (Back Bone Interface) unit to indicate that it has a completion command to send. The
state machine (vrbrwc_fsm) of VRBMU completion handling fub (vrbrwc) goes from IDLE to Completion Request state (COMPLR).
BBI unit will assert req with the appropriate request attributes to gain ownership of the IOSF bus. The req_cget is asserted to the
VRBMU after the IOSF bus ownership is granted (gnt). The vrbrwc_fsm then goes to transaction containing data state to wait for
transaction cget and dget.
When the IOSF Fabric decides to perform ‘Transaction Command’, the cget is asserted to inform VRBMU that the command is already
taken. At the same clock, it will also forward the address and header information up the IOSF bus via BBI and the data cycle (dput
assertion) will be followed.
Figure: VRunit Non-Posted Cycles and their Completions shows examples of a non-posted read and a non-posted write cycles.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 171/242
=== PAGE 172 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
Figure: VRunit Non-Posted Cycles and their Completions
25.1.1.2.4 VRunit MSI request cycle
SATA MSI request cycles are initiated by VRunit.
A MSI request transaction starts with VRBMU asserting req_avail to BBI to indicate that it has a command to send. The transaction of
an MSI cycle is similar to a completion cycle except for the request type, cpid and rqid content.
For a completion cycle, the cpid is from the strapped busdevfunction number and rqid is used from the registered rqid from the non-
posted request. The request type is completion.
For an MSI cycle, the rqid is from strapped bus-dev-function numbers. The request type is posted.
25.1.1.3 Bus Number Latching
The bus-number latching for a SATA controller is done in VRBDEC fub of VRunit. The SATA controller latches the downstream bus-
number only if two conditions below are TRUE.
Condition (1) Configuration-type-0 WRITE Cycles
Condition (2) The WRITE cycle is error-free
25.1.1.4 Summary of cycles supported by VRunit
The VRunit is responsible for decoding each backbone cycle to identify the cycle belonging to the SATA cluster and claiming the cycle.
The cycles it supports include configuration, memory and IO types as shown in Table: Cycle Supported by VRunit.
NOTE: The memory mapped registers within the SATA controller exist in non-cacheable memory space. Additionally, locked accesses
are not supported. If software attempts to perform locked transactions to the registers, indeterminate results can occur.
Register accesses have amaximum size of 64 bits. 64 bit accesses must not cross an 8Byte alignment boundary.
Refer to IOSF 0.82 specification for exact format and type field encoding.
Table: Cycle Supported by VRunit (source)
Cycle
Description

IORd IO Read Request.
IOWr IO Write Request.
CfgRd0 Configuration Read Type 0.
CfgWr0 Configuration Write Type 0.

Completion without data. Used for IO and Configuration write completions.
Cpl Backbone devices never use this for memory read completions, but it is used by the completion queue to the upstream transaction layer
with memory read completion status other than “Successful Completion”.
CplD Completion with Data. Used for Memory, IO, and Configuration Read Completions.
MSI 32-bit Memory Write Request.
The VRunit has one device function controller to support up to 8 SATA ports. The SoC driven istrap_c1_DeviceNumber0[4:0] and
istrap_c1_FunctionNumber0[2:0] is used to identify the device number and function number of the controller. This controller is capable
of claiming configuration cycles, memory cycles and IO cycles.
Cycles that are claimed are either completed or trapped out. VRunit is responsible for generating the completion request for all the
cycles that it claims. For trapped IO cycles, the completion request will only be sent to BB once the SMI generation handshake with
Wunit is done.
25.1.1.5 VRunit and Intra-Unit Cycle Flows and Policies and Logic Structure and Sequence
Diagrams
There are two types of handshake protocols between VRunit and VSunit/VSAunit for IO accesses to the SATA cluster. Specifically, IO
accesses to:
Shadow taskfile (without trapping-enabled) are done with the sel/sel_ack protocol.
Port-specific memory registers are done with either AIDP or SIDP protocols.
Figure: VRunit-VSunit sel/sel-ack Protocol Handshake
The protocol works as follows:
sel is asserted by VRLIF to initiate cycle forwarding to VS. sel cannot be asserted if sel_ack is detected asserted by VRLIF.
sel is deasserted when the “sel_ack” is detected to be asserted by VRLIF.
sel_ack is asserted by VSunit when it detects “sel” to be asserted.
sel_ack is deasserted by VSunit when “sel” is detected to be deasserted.
addr and wr are driven by VRLIF. These signals are valid whenever sel is asserted.
during write, VRVSdata is driven by VRLIF and it is valid whenever sel is asserted.
during read, VSVRdata is driven by VSunit and it is valid wenever sel_ack is asserted.
There are two pairs of sel/sel-ack used due to different targets.
VRVSsel, VSVRsel_ack is associated with access to the ATA task file registers which is located in the Transport Layer. Transport
layer runs to bb_cclk.
VRVSbmsel, VSVRbmsel_ack is associated with access to Bus Master registers which are located in the DMA Engine. DMA Engine
runs at bb_clk.
When a write cycle is forwarded, VRLIF asserts VRVSsel, provides the address, write and data to VSunit and waits for a sel_ack from
VSunit as a confirmation that the write has been successful. VRunit then returns completion upstream to CPU for the write cycle.
When a read cycle is forwarded, VRLIF asserts VRVSsel, provides the address and read to VSunit and waits for a sel_ack and data from
VSunit as a confirmation that the read has been successful. VRunit then returns completion upstream to CPU for the read cycle.

25.1.1.6 AHCI Index-Data Pair Interface
The AHCI Index/Data mechanism allows access to the AHCI memory mapped registers through an indexing scheme that resides in the
I/O space. The Index and Data registers must be accessed in DWord, Word, or Byte granularity.
The index/data pair interface to VSunit consists of a 5-bit address bus: VRVSixaddr[6:2], a write pulse for each port, activated at data
phase: VRVS#ixput, VRVSbeB[3:0], VS#VRbmdata and VRVSdata.
Figure: VRunit-VSunit Index/Data Pair Interface
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 175/242
=== PAGE 176 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
Memory cycle access via VSBMU and memory cycle access via the Index/Data pair mechanism supported by VRunit can never occur at
the same time. VRunit always hascontrol over the memory registers unless there is a memory cycle targeting VSBMU.
The protocol works as follows:
VRunit places VRVSbeB and VRVSixaddr on the interface when it has a memory register access via index/data pair mechanism to
forward to the VSunit.
For memory registers write VRVS#ixput will be asserted high and data will be placed on the bus on the same cycle as the valid
address.
For memory reads, VRVS#ixput will be low, and data from VSunit is valid on the bus on the same cycle as the valid address
Figure: Index/Data Pair Cycle for Memory Register Access
The Index register is located at offset h10 from the AIDPBA Base, and the data register is located at offset h14
Table: AHCI Index-Data Pair Registers
Offset Bits [31:16] Bits [15:0]
h10 INDEX INDEX
h14 DATA DATA
The data register is byte accessible during a write. During a read, all 32 bits of data will be placed on the bus. The upper DW will return
all h0000. Access to Index/data is limited to 1 DW at a time. NO simultaneous index+data write is permitted since index is defined as
DW wide, data is also DW wide, I/O is max to 1 DW.

25.1.1.7.2 MSI
The MSI feature is only supported in the AHCI SKU and the RAID SKU. Only Controller 1 supports the MSI feature. Controller 1 supports
only one MSI mode, Single MSI.
Single-MSI
The setting of the number of messages to use is governed by the Controller 1 config reg MC.MME and the MC.MMC fields. Controller 1
always reverts to the single-MSI mode if its MC.MME != MC.MMC. Because Multiple Message Enable (MME) and Multiple Message
Capable (MMC) are RO zero, only Single-MSI is supported.
25.1.2 VSAUNIT
25.1.2.1 VSAUNIT–Bus Master Unit (BMU)
The VSBMU manages all of the VSUNIT memory transactions in relation to the IOSF. This includes buffering and alignment of memory
cycles that originate from the VSAUNIT or those claimed by VSAUNIT. Although other accesses are supported by the IOSF specification,
VSAUNIT only uses two request cycle types, namely memory write requests and memory read requests. SATA configuration and I/O
request cycles are handled by the VRUNIT.
In addition to cycles originating from the CPU, VSBMU also supports peer accesses, such as memory accesses targeting VSAUNIT
from other IOSF agents. VSAUNITs do not initiate peer accesses.
As an initiator of memory request cycles for VSAUNIT, VSABMU performs the following memory read requests on the behalf of DMA:
Data fetch
PRD (Physical Region Descriptor) fetch
Command List fetch
Command FIS (CFIS) fetch / re-fetch
ATAPI command (ACMD) fetch
CL/PIOS fetch
BCU fetch
Similarly, VSBMU performs the following memory write request for DMA:
Data posting
Received FIS posting
VSABMU also performs a Byte Count Update (BCU) through an upstream memory write request. As part of the AHCI requirement, BCU
is a count of the data transferred on the IOSF immediately prior to it.
VSABMU also responds to memory cycles targeting VSAUNIT.
For downstream memory read cycles addressed to VSAUNIT, VSABMU is responsible for returning the memory read completion.
For memory writes to VSAUNIT (specifically VSADMA), VSABMU presents the data from IOSF together with the address and length
information to the DMA Engine.
VSABMU interfaces with the IOSF through an IOSF Interface (VSAI) unit that basically translates the IOSF protocol into a SiBus
compliant protocol. The purpose of this is to reduce the redesign effort required during the change from SPXB to IOSF during CPT.
SiBus protocol uses simple get-put protocol that reduces communication complexity.
This protocol translation occurs in the VSBSI fub, which is described in more details in the following section.
VSABMU has three virtual request pipes to the IOSF through the VSAI unit (Table: VSABMU–Three Virtual Request Pipes). These
request pipes are chosen based on ordering requirements with descending traffic density (Pipe 1 has the highest traffic density). But, all
the request pipes share the primary interface and channel.
Table: VSABMU–Three Virtual Request Pipes (source)
Pipe Usage
Used mainly for DMA data read/write transfer.
Pipe 1 All write requests from VSAUNIT use this pipe, including BCU and FIS posting.
This ensures the ordering requirement is met, where FIS posting must not pass the preceding BCU.
Dedicated for Command List and PRD fetch using memory read requests.
Pipe 2
There is no data path for pipe 2 requests.
Solely used for memory register read completions.
Pipe 3
This guarantees that downstream reads are not blocked by any host controller operations.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 177/242
=== PAGE 178 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
Figure: BMU Data Flow
BMU Data Flow
For both upstream and downstream DMA data transfers, the data is first passed through the Data Formatter FUB within the VSBMU
(i.e. VSBDATF), before being stored in the SRAM through the VSBDBUF FUB.
As shown in Figure: BMU Data Flow, both data retrieved from the IOSF and data from the DMA are aligned to the 2QW boundary before
being stored in the SRAM to increase the buffer usage to the fullest.
NOTE: A non-functional data path from the DMA directly to the SRAM exists. This path allows the use of BERT mode testing.
Received FIS and CFIS Fetch
The Received FIS from VST is passed directly to the BMU buffer as it is already QW aligned. The same applies for the CFIS data. As the
CFIS data are always properly aligned, BMU passes the CFIS received directly to VST without performing any alignment.
PRD Fetch
The PRD data retrieved from the IOSF is first right aligned within the PRD Formatter FUB before being passed to the DMA Engine. As
there is no storage element in the PRD Formatter FUB, the PRD data is directly written into the DMA PRD Queues.
Memory Register Write and Read
Upon detection of a memory register write request from IOSF, BMU asserts vsBDregput as a single pulse signal to DMA. During the
assertion of vsBDregput, the memory address and data to be written is also valid (vsBDregaddr and vsBDrpcdata). This data is flopped
into DMA on the next clock. The memory register data is then available on the following rising edge.
For memory register reads, BMU passes to DMA the register location of the requested read (vsBDregaddr). The requested data should
be valid when vsBDregaddr indicates the location of the memory register.
25.1.2.1.1 Detailed Memory Request Cycles Supported by VSBMU
1. Data Fetch
BMU launches these requests only if it has sufficient storage for its completion. This includes additional storage provided by the MUX
unit (if the sync fifo is exposed). Request commands from DMA Engine can be large and thus are broken down to multiple smaller
requests by BMU. BMU keeps track of the address and remaining length within itself. These data are word aligned and tracking is
required to align their completion into DWords. DMA data read and DMA data write can never occur at the same time.
These requests have “00” in the least significant bits (LSB) of their tag field using the request pipe 1. Any request length longer than 256
Bytes is broken by the VSBSI unit into 256 Byte packets to match the maximum buffer size in NVBMEMUNIT. To achieve optimum
performance, the request length is broken into 64Byte packets.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 178/242
=== PAGE 179 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
2. Data Posting
DMA data writes have a similar alignment to the DMA data read, but are split at 64 Byte boundaries, instead of 256 Bytes. This
maximum size is inherited from ICH5. There is no additional visibility for the BMU on data availability, other than those residing in the
BMU SRAM. The BMU launches write requests with the length it tracks within itself when it has sufficient data to post or there is a flush
directive from the DMA Engine unless the stop directive is also present. These requests have “00” in the least significant bits of their tag
field using request pipe 1.
3. ATAPI Command (ACMD) Fetch
The ATAPI command reads are treated as any other DMA data read requests and are transparent to BMU. However, it is handled
differently in the MUX unit. These reads are usually shorter compared to the DMA data reads.
4. Received FIS Posting
The BMU takes the data for these cycles directly from the VST unit and issues a take signal to the transport unit without any alignment
on it. However, The length and address information is taken from the DMA Engine. These requests are made through pipe 1 and have
“00” in the least significant bits of their tag field. The received FIS are differentiated from the DMA data with different token values
(Data=00, Rx FIS=01).
5. Command FIS (CFIS) fetch
This fetch commands (RH2D FISes) to be sent to the device. It is done through pipe 1. Because their completions have to be directed to
a different storage (VST) they have a different tag which is “01”. No alignment is done on its completions. Command FISes reside in the
command table and are not expected to cross the 4k boundary. The command FIS fetch should only be issued by the DMA Engine
before any DMA data transfer starts. The length of the CFIS is based on the CFL value in the Command Table.
6. Byte Count Update (BCU)
BCU is a count of the data transferred on the IOSF immediately prior to it. The initial intention of BCU is to report the exact count of data
reaching the target at the earliest possible time considering the BCU request is not be granted until the active transfer for the current
active PRD is finished.
The BCU for data in and data out is treated differently. For data in, the BCU is tracked by the BMU and data out the BCU is tracked by the
Transport.
For data-in:
For data in, the BCU is tracked in by the BMU when the data is posted to the memory. Because the specification requires that the BCU
posted to memory should not include the data that have not gotten an R_OK. As the data FIS is 8K, there is no way to keep all the data in
before it gets the R_OK. To meet this requirement the BMU actually keeps 2 counters, bibcount, which track the total data byte count
that is posted to memory. While another counter named “subtract” tracks how many byte counts of data get posted to memory (data
that have not gotten R_OK).
Conceptually the BCU = bibcount - subtract
The update value is not always accurate until the completion of a command.
For data-out:
For data out, the BCU is triggered by vsaT when the vsaT sends out data FIS and gets R_OK. vsaT sends a pulse signal,
vsTBpayldfistxok, to BMU along with a byte count value, vsTBpayldfistx_cnt. The pulse signal enables BMU to perform the byte count
update with the byte count value, which indicates the number of data bytes that are successfully received by the device. The update
value is always accurate to the number reported by vsaT. If the total transfer count of a command exceeds the maximum length of a
data FIS (8KB), then the BMU updates the byte count in 8 KB granulites as each 8 KB data FIS is sent and gets the R_OK. If the total
transfer count of a command or the remaining transfer count is less than 8KB, the BMU updates the exact count upon the completion of
the command.
7. Memory Register Read Completion
Upon seeing a downstream read request from the IOSF, the BMU initiates a read completion request through pipe 3. Information, such
as length, is kept within the BMU. The lower bit of the address is not kept. This information is then presented to the DMA Engine where
the registers reside. The BMU alters the address to the the DMA Engine dynamically during the data phase. The host controller must not
be in the D3 power state and the memory space must be enabled for the BMU to respond to register read requests. However, the AHCI
enable bit is not considered. These requests have “00” in the least significant bits of their tag field on pipe 3.
NOTE: The operation of returning completion must not be disrupted by a host reset. Thus, the backbone reset is used in this part of the
logic.
8. PRD Fetch
The BMU launches a PRD fetch request through pipe 2. This is performed in both AHCI and legacy modes, with the only difference being
the length of the PRD. The BMU does not need to know if it is in AHCI mode because the length is indicated by the DMA engine. The
BMU breaks the requests into two if they cross 4k page boundaries (for legacy only). PRDs are expected to be DWord aligned. These
requests have “10” in the least significant bits of their tag field and use request pipe 2.
9. Command List Fetch
The command list fetch is equivalent to the PRD fetch as far as the BMU is concerned. The only difference is their length. The
command lists are also DWord aligned.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 179/242
