25.1.2.2 VSAUNIT - DMA Unit
The SATA controller supports the AHCI mode. Non-data transfer, ATA PIO read, ATA PIO write, ATA DMA read, ATA DMA write, ATAPI,
and queued command transfer are supported in AHCI. In the AHCI, the PIO transfer is completed through the DMA transfer in terms of
the DMA engine and the Bus Management Unit interfaces.
The DMA engine is responsible for fetching the command list in the system memory depending on the PxCI[z] bit. Upon receiving the
command list, it starts fetching the command FIS, the ATAPI command if applicable, and the PRD entries. Upon receiving the PRDs, the
DMA engine starts fetching data from the system memory or posting data to the system memory if data from the device are available.
The AHCI port-specific, memory register block resides in the DMA engine. The DMA engine is responsible for the PRD interrupt
generation and the device interrupt generation by looking at the corresponding status and enables the bit in the memory register.
The DMA Engine receives the FIS write request to the BMU when the RX FIS queue in the Transport layer is available. The DMA Engine
provides the byte-count update address to the BMU.
Figure: DMA Engine Data, Address and Command Path
CI Bit Checking
The DMA engine only runs when the PxCMD.ST (START ) bit is set. Upon the ST bit set, the DMA Engine starts at location 0 of the
PxCI.CI field. The DMA engine checks every CI bit in the PxCI register and determines which one is set at every clock.
The DMA engine executes the command list entry if the CI bit is set. The DMA Engine may only advance to another command list entry
when PxTFD.STS[BSY], PxTFD.STS[DRQ], and PxTFD.STS[ERR] are all cleared. The DMA engine executes commands where the next
available PxCI.CI[z] bit is set, relative to its current position. For example, if the command list pointer is at entry “0”, then PxCI.CI[1] has
highest priority, followed by PxCI.CI[2], PxCI.CI[3], etc. If the command pointer is at entry “30”, then PxCI.CI[31] has highest priority,
followed by PxCI.CI[0], PxCI.CI[1], etc., with PxCI.CI[29] having lowest priority.
When the DMA engine finds the highest priority entry, it moves its command pointer to that slot entry, sets the BSY bit, and processes
that command. When this command is finished, the process begins again.
If no CI bit is set, the DMA Engine pauses at its current location and still checks all CI bits at every clock.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 181/242
=== PAGE 182 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
ST Bit
The DMA engine only starts its operation if the ST bit is set. If the ST bit is cleared, the DMA Engine asserts the vsDBstop and waits for
the stop acknowledge from the BMU. The BMU acknowledges the stop signal from the DMA engine if no read completion is pending.
The DMA engine generates a reset after getting the stop acknowledge.
Data Transfer
The DMA data transfer does not pass through the DMA engine, only the handshake signal is controlled by the DMA.
DMA Write or PIO Read Data Transfer Handshake
During DMA Write or PIO Read, the MUX Unit asserts the vsMDdgive signal together with the high and low double-word enable and data
signals, vsMDhavail, vsMDlavail (low and high dword enable) and vsMBdata[63:0]. Then the DMA engine translates this into
vsDBweB[3:0] based on the PRD count and asserts the vsDBdgive to the BMU one clock after the vsMDdgive. The BMU issues a “take”
by asserting the vsBDdtake. The DMA engine issues another data “give” to the BMU if not all of the valid data given by the MUX unit has
been transfer to the BMU. The DMA engine ensures that all of the valid data given by the MUX unit has been transferred to BMU and
only asserts the vsDMdtake when the last piece of data is taken by BMU.
NOTE: The DMA engine generates the vsDMlast and vsDMweB[3:0] signal when the remaining DMA data is less than or equal to a quad
word. The MUX unit issues the last transfer when the data is ready. It uses the word enable from the DMA engine if the available data is
more than the word enable indicated by DMA Engine. (This is due to the natural data size of SATA is in dword.)
DMA Read or PIO Write Data Transfer Handshake
For DMA read or PIO write, the BMU asserts the vsBDdgive signal and drives vsBMparerr and vsBMdata[63:0] to the MUX Unit. The DMA
engine generates the word-enable signal vsDMweB[3:0] and the vsDMtag interrupt-tag signal to the MUX unit and then asserts the
vsDMdgive one clock after the vsBDdgive assert. The MUX unit can take the data by asserting the vsMDdtake signal any time after the
vsDMdgive is active. The DMA engine asserts the vsDBdtake right at the same cycle as the vsMDdtake.
In the case of the cross PRD boundary, where the 64 bits of data belong to two PRD, the DMA engine expires the current PRD count and
loads the next PRD count and then only asserts the 64-bit data give to the MUX unit.
In the case of multiple small PRDs, the BMU Data Formatter needs to have more than two PRDs data fetch to right align the “data in” to
a quad word format. This requires the DMA engine to expire the first few PRD count events when the BMU has not asserted the
vsBDdgive. The DMA engine only asserts the vsDMdgive when the BMU has one or more quad words of data.
DMA Engine Read Request Interface with BMU
The DMA engine submits five types of read requests: Command List fetch, Command FIS fetch, PRD fetch, ATAPI command fetch, and
DMA read request to BMU. The DMA engine asserts the vsDBrcgive signal whenever there is pending Command List fetch, Command
FIS fetch, DMA read, or PRD fetch requests. The address, length and request type are driven on vsDBraddr[63:0], vsDBrlen[15:1], and
vsDBrtype[1:0] at the same cycle as the vsDBrcgive signal asserted.
The BMU can issue the request by asserting the vsBDrctake signal anytime after the vsDBrcgive is asserted.The DMA engine does not
switch requests until the current request is taken by the BMU.
DMA Engine DMA Write Request Interface with BMU
The DMA engine issues a write request to the BMU through a give-and-take interface. For the DMA Write request, when the DMA Engine
has the DMA-write PRD information ready, it drives the DMA write request address, length on vsDBwaddr[63:0], vsDBwlen[15:1] and
asserts the vsDBwcgive signal.
The BMU can take the request by asserting the vsBDwctake signal anytime after the vsDBwcgive signal is asserted. The DMA request
information is not altered after the vsDBwcgive is asserted or until the vsBDwctake is asserted.
NOTE: The BMU asserts vsBDwctake when the BMU SRAM is not empty and does not wait for all micro commands for the macro
command to be sent out.
RX FIS Write Request Interface with BMU
For the RX FISwrite request: when the transport layer indicates to the DMA engine there is a FIS available for posting and the FIS type,
the DMA Engine drives the Rx FIS write request address, length on vsDBfisaddr[63:0], vsDBfislen[6:2] and asserts the vsDBfiscgive
signal.
The BMU can take the request by asserting the vsBDfisctake signal anytime after the vsDBfiscgive signal is asserted. The DMA request
information is not altered after the vsDBfiscgive is asserted or until the vsBDfisctake is asserted.
The reason to have 2 separate command queues for the Rx FIS and the DMA Write is that the Write Request Command can be quite
long, up to the length of the PRD. This cannot block the Rx FIS posting for the entire duration of the PRD. Assume that two PRDs are
fetched for the DMA write, 4K each. The DMA presents the first 4K write and the BMU accepts. Now, the DMA presents the next 4K
write. At this point we do not want to be prevented from sending an Rx FIS posting (can be due to error condition). Rx FIS should not be
blocked.
In the BMU, the DMA Write request always has a higher priority than the Rx FIS write request.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 182/242
=== PAGE 183 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
25.1.2.3 VSAUNIT - Transport and MUX
The transport layer houses the shadowed taskfile, the legacy interrupt pending flag, and emulates the necessary protocols to support
legacy ATA/ATAPI protocols (described in the serialATA specification) and to be compatible with the legacy device drivers. The
transport layer constructs all FISs to be transmitted and initiates them to the MUX unit. The transport layer parses all FISs received, and
takes appropriate action.
The transport layer also houses the AHCI Signature memory-mapped register. For the AHCI, the transport layer provides the DMA
engine with AHCI status bits, the type of non-Data FIS received, the length and its entire content unaltered, including the FIS header. In
addition, for queued command operation, the transport layer passes the “tag” field ontained from the DMA Setup FIS to the DMA
Engine.
AHCI requires a failed H2D Reg FIS to be resent automatically by the host hardware until the FIS is accepted by a device with R_OK.
The purpose of the MUX unit is to MUX the transmit datapath between the transport layer and the DMA-data datapath from the
BMU/DMA Engine. During receptions, the MUX unit generates the FIS reception indication signals to the transport layer. The MUX unit
also houses the HOLD-HOLDA latency buffer required by the specification. This buffer is used during PIO data-in transfers, and DMA
data-in transfers. The same buffer is reused during DMA data-out transfers. The buffer is not used during legacy PIO data
transmissions.
For AHCI, the buffer is used for all AHCI DMA or PIO data operations, both transmission and reception. This includes the ATAPI
Command, which is handled similarly to PIO Write Data FIS with the additional ability of discarding some pre-fetched data from the
ATAPI Packet memory space.
In addition, the BMU might have visibility into the syncFIFO storage space during both AHCI and legacy modes. Associated with
exposing syncFIFO visibility to BMU is that syncFIFO is capable of accepting data from BMU prior to reception of DMA Activate FIS (for
both legacy/AHCI) or PIO Setup FIS (for AHCI only). There are 3 modes of operations.
Mode 1: BMU has no visibility at all and MUX unit would not accept data from BMU until reception of PIO Setup FIS (AHCI PIO
Read/Write) or DMA Activate FIS (legacy/AHCI DMA-Read).
Mode 2: The visibility is exposed only after reception of PIO Setup FIS or DMA Activate FIS.
Mode 3: The visibility is always exposed to the BMU and the MUX unit does not wait for the PIO Setup FIS arrival and holds off the
BMU data until then (same as in legacy mode, holding off the BMU data until DMA Activate as showen above).
Mode 2 is the default.
Logically, the transport layer initiates all transmissions to the MUX unit. The MUX unit then MUXes between the transport layer initiated
transmissions and DMA data from the BMU, and sends them to the link layer. During reception, the MUX unit receives all data from the
link layer and sends them either upto the transport layer, or to the BMU, for DMA data. However, even for DMA data receptions, controls
are still sent to the transport layer for tracking. Similarly, for DMA data tranmissions, the MUX unit provides information to the transport
layer for tracking. This is used particularly to segment data FISs into 8kB chunks, because the transport layer ultimately decides when a
FIS transmission begins and ends. From a hardware perspective, the datapaths and control logic can be classified as non-data, PIO, and
DMA.
25.1.2.3.1 Nondata Command Cycle Walkthough and General Transmission/Reception Protocol
All commands are issued by writing to the command register in the taskfile. The taskfile interfaces to the router unit via a sel, sel_ack
protocol. This protocol uses a 4-phase handshake to transfer data between VRunit and transport layer logic.
Figure: Router Read of Taskfile (sel_ack_read.vsd)
Figure: Router Write to Taskfile (sel_ack_write.vsd)
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 183/242
=== PAGE 184 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
Whenever the target samples sel are active, the cycle is a read cycle, it places the data onto the data bus, and assert sel_ack. When the
target samples sel as deasserted, this indicates that the router has already sampled the data, and the target might now change the data
bus. In the actual implementation, the data is driven together with sel_ack. For writes, when the target samples sel as active, the target
latches in the value on the data bus, and simultaneously asserts sel_ack.
First, the command parameters are written. There is no assumed ordering between writes to the DEV bit in the device/head register,
versus writes to any other non-command register. Only the command register must be written last.
There is dedicated storage space for the taskfile. When the command register is written, the BSY bit is set by the transport layer
immediately, so that an immediate subsequent read-back of the status register shows BSY=1. The transport layer gates the
transmission of a H2D Register FIS with DEV=0. The transport layer performs master-only emulation, and the responsibility of slave
emulation falls on the router. If DEV=0, then the transport layer initiates a FIS transmission to the MUX unit.
The MUX unit will passes this unmodified to the link layer. The signals commanding a transmission are: vsTMfisstart, vsTMfislast,
vsTMdgive, vsTMd[31:0]. These are passed unmodified, in this case, by the MUX unit to the link layer as vsMLfisstart, vsMLfislast,
vsMLdgive, vsMLd[31:0]. The link layer indicates acceptance of each dword by asserting vsLMdtake, which is passed up the transport
layer as vsMTdtake. This follows the standard give-take protocol. vsMLfisstart is an envelope signal indicating that all transmissions
under this umbrella are meant to be under a single FIS. vsMLfislast indicates that this is the last dword of the FIS transmission, and that
the link layer should close the FIS by inserting the CRC dword, followed by the EOF primitive. A subsequent assertion of vsMLfisstart
would indicate a new H2D FIS.
Figure: Transmission Protocol to Link Layer from MUX Unit (vsMLtx.vsd)
Following the transmission of any H2D FIS (and not just the H2D Register FIS), there is always a handshake from the link layer back to
the transport layer to indicate whether the FIS was received by the device successfully or not, as indicated by the RERR or ROK primitive
received during the FIS handshake phase.
The status is qualified by vsLTtxstat, which qualifies vsLTtxerr and vsLTtxlinkerr. The former indicates that an RERR primitive was
received from the device indicating a CRC error, while the latter indicates that at some point during the FIS transmission, the device
failed to follow proper link layer protocol.
These transitions, due to unexpected primitive reception, are defined in the serialATA specification. Upon encountering an error, the
transport layer does not retransmit any FIS, relying instead on software recovery actions, specifically the DEVICE_RESET command and
software reset action. The transport layer acknowledges the status by asserting vsTLstat_ack. Until this signal asserts, the link layer
keeps vsLTtxstat asserted. This forms a handshake pair that serves to synchronize the actions of the link and transport layer. Every
transmission results in a status from the link layer which must be acknowledged by the transport layer. While waiting for the
acknowledgement, the link layer sends the WTRM primitive.
Because the transport layer always waits for vsLTtxstat for any FIS transmission, it will not initiate a new H2D FIS until the status is
returned by the link layer. Therefore, no back-to-back FIS transmissions are possible. There will always be a period of intervening clocks
as the host waits for the device acknowledgement. The link layer handles the transmission or reception of one FIS at a time, and the
transport layer and MUX unit cannot pipeline more than one FIS into the link layer. The link layer is also not cognizant of the FIS type
being transmitted. It merely obeys the command to transmit a FIS of arbitrary length from the MUX unit.
When the device has completed the actions for the non-data command, it returns a D2H Register FIS that updates the taskfile, clears
BSY, and potentially sets the interrupt, if the I bit is set. The link layer indicates the reception of a D2H FIS via vsLM_syncfifo_we,
vsLM_set_F, vsLM_set_L.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 184/242
=== PAGE 185 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
Figure: Reception Protocol from Link Layer to MUX Unit (vsLMrx.vsd)
The vsLM_syncfifo_we indicates that the link layer is writing a dword into the MUX unit or transport layer. This write is unconditional and
cannot be flow-controlled. This may or may not assert on every Lclk. However, the worst assumption, that the link layer will produce a
dword every Lclk, is used. In reality, because the bandwidth of the link layer at Lclk=66 MHz is much higher than the serialATA
bandwidth, a dword is produced only at least one every other clock. This ultimately depends on the relationship between Lclk and the
serialATA speed. vsLM_set_F indicates that this is the first dword in the FIS and vsLM_set_L indicates that this is the last dword of the
FIS.
Note that vsLM_syncfifo_we may or may not result in the syncFIFO being written into. The syncFIFO is not used for control FISs,
meaning short FISs of known length. These include the D2H Register FIS, D2H Pio_Setup FIS, D2H Dma_Activate FIS, D2H Set-Device-
Bits FIS and D2H Bist_Activate FIS. The MUX unit decodes the FIS header, which resides in the first dword of the received FIS, and
generates the FIS reception control signals to the transport layer. These are indicated by vsMTfisstart, vsMTfislast, vsMLd[31:0],
vsMTdgive. There is a handshake signal vsTMdtake that is only used for reception of non-control FISs.
Figure: Reception Protocol from MUX Unit to Transport Layer (vsMTrx.vsd)
Control FISs are written immediately to the transport layer, on the same clock that vsLM_syncfifo_we asserts. There is no storage for
them other than what the transport layer provides. Hence, the transport layer must decode the header when vsMTfisstart and
vsMTdgive assert, and assert vsTMdtake on the same clock. For the reception of a D2H Register FIS, the transport layer writes the FIS
contents into the taskfile. The handshake signal vsTMdtake is only used during PIO data reads (receptions), and during the reception of
unrecognized FISs, that is, FISs with an unrecognized header. Both are discussed later.
Similar to the transmission protocol, the link layer provides the reception status on vsLTrxstat, vsLTrxerr and vsLTrxlinkerr. If the link
layer verifies the CRC to be incorrect, vsLTrxerr is asserted. If the link layer encounters a link layer protocol during reception,
vsLTrxlinkerr is asserted. Both these signals are qualified by vsLTrxstat. The transport layer acknowledges the status by asserting
vsTLstat_ack, which forms a handshake. The FIS reception is not complete until the handhshake occurs. This serves to synchronize the
actions ot the link and transport layer.
The transport layer also has the option of asserting vsTLstat_fail together with vsTLstat_ack. This has the effect of causing the link
layer to return the RERR primitive during the handshake phase of the FIS. This is used by the transport layer to fail the reception of
unexpected FISs, causing a violation in the transport layer protocol, even if the CRC was verified to be correct, and there had been no
other link layer level error. However, if the link layer already detects a CRC error, or some other link layer level error, then the RERR
primitive is returned, regardless of vsTLstat_fail.
The reception of a D2H Register FIS will cause the contents of the register FIS to be loaded into the taskfile. The taskfile is not loaded
immediately, but a set of holding registers are updated first. When the CRC is verified to be correct, the actual taskfile is updated, so that
it is now visible to software. If the CRC is incorrect, as indicated by vsLTrxerr, then the update does not occur. A similar action occurs for
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 185/242
=== PAGE 186 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
the interrupt pending flag (IPF), and its associated holding bit. An interrupt is asserted if the IPF is set, DEV=0, and nIEN=0. A software
read of the status register clears the IPF, as per the serialATA spec. The device can never clear the IPF.
The reception of an unrecognized FIS is treated similarly. The transport layer must issue vsTMdtake on every clock vsMTdgive that is
asserted (which is simultaneuous with vsLM_syncfifo_we) and throws the unrecognized FIS away.
The reception of a D2H Data FIS during dma data-in, will cause vsMTdgive to be asserted for every dword that the link layer produces.
However, only the header is taken by the transport layer with vsTMdtake. The rest of the data is taken unconditionally by the MUX unit’s
syncFIFO. However, vsMTdgive will still appear to be asserted. When the final dword is written into the syncFIFO, vsMTlast is asserted,
signalling to the transport layer that this FIS has been completely received.
25.1.2.3.2 Legacy PIO Cycle Walkthrough
The transport layer receives notification of an impending PIO operation by the reception of a D2H Pio_Setup FIS. This FIS places the
transport layer in a state to sequence PIO control.
25.1.2.3.2.1 PIO Reads
For PIO reads, the reception of the D2H Pio_Setup FIS, which has a direction bit, causes the transport layer to output a state signal
vsTM_HTPS6_PIOITrans1 to the MUX unit, so that a subsequent D2H Data FIS will cause the MUX unit to respond correctly. The
syncFIFO then doles out the PIO data, 1 dword at a time, to the transport layer, using the same FIS reception protocol between the MUX
unit and the transport layer. The transport layer is not cognizant of whether data originated from the syncFIFO or not. The only
difference now is that the transport layer may flow-control the data by deasserting vsTMdtake if software has not yet read the dword
from the dataport. The arrival of the D2H Data FIS causes the transport layer to update the contents of the status register with the initial
status field in the Pio_Setup FIS. This causes software to see that the data is ready, and will start reading from the dataport. The
incoming data is pushed into the dataport, 1 dword at a time. Software may mix word and dword reads, as long as a dword read always
occurs after an even number of word reads. When the final dword has been read from the taskfile, the transport layer immediately
updates the status register with the ending status in the Pio_Setup FIS. Software need not wait 400ns to read the status register. The
transport layer tracks this by decrementing the count in the Pio_Setup FIS. The count is decremented upon a read by software.
During reception of the D2H Data FIS, if the high watermark is reached in the syncFIFO, the MUX unit will assert
vsML_syncfifo_high_wtrmk to the link layer, which causes the latter to send the HOLD primitive to the device. The device may then
continue to send a worst case of 20 dwords of data before pausing. When the low watermark is reached, the MUX unit unholds the
burst by asserting vsML_syncfifo_low_wtrmk.
NOTE: The high watermark signal must have first deasserted prior to asserting the low watermark signal.
The syncFIFO is designed to a have a depth that can carry the worst case throttling latency, which must include the time to detect a
high watermark, and the time to send the HOLD primitive, while continuing to absorb all data in the pipeline that is already in the link
layer. (The 20 dwords refers to the device side of the interface only).
25.1.2.3.2.2 PIO Writes
For PIO writes, the reception of a D2H Pio_Setup FIS, which has a direction bit, causes the transport layer to update the initial status
from the Pio_Setup FIS into the status register. This causes software to begin writing to the dataport. Software may mix any number of
word writes and dword writes, as long as the final amount of data written matches the transfer count in the Pio_Setup FIS. The
transport layer performs the necessary realignment. Each write by software decrements the count. When the final word or dword is
written by software, the transport layer immediately updates the status register with the ending status value from the Pio_Setup FIS. As
in the read case, software need not wait 400ns to read the status register.
The writes by software are concatenated into a single H2D Data FIS, and transmitted by the transport layer to the MUX unit, as per the
transport layer-MUX unit interface. The MUX unit is not cognizant of what FIS is being transmitted and merely passes these signals to
the link layer. The PIO transmit data do not go through the syncFIFO.
25.1.2.3.3 Legacy DMA Cycle Walkthrough
For a DMA data-in transfer, the transport layer receives a notification that a D2H Data FIS has been received, while the transport layer is
in the IDLE state. This determines that the D2H Data FIS can only correspond to a DMA operation, because a PIO operation requires a
prior reception of the D2H Pio_Setup FIS. The bus-master start bit vsDTstartdma cannot be asserted at this point, because software
may have issued the DMA command to the device prior to starting the DMA engine.
The D2H Data FIS header by the transport layer is taken on the same clock that the vsLM_syncfifo_we asserts and is not written into the
syncFIFO. The payload data, however, is written into the syncFIFO and is presented to the DMA engine in the Uclk125 domain, one
qword at a time. Whenever a dword is available, vsMDldwavail is asserted. Whenever both dwords are valid, vsMDhdwavail and
vsMDldwavail are asserted. Both are qualified by vsMDdgive. The DMA engine indicates its reception of data via vsDMdtake. The flow
control mechanism is similar to the PIO reads, that is, via the low and high watermarks.
The reception of a D2H Register FIS, while there is data outstanding in the syncFIFO, indicates that the device is done with the DMA
data-in transfer. The device can also segment the D2H Data FISs into any intermediate size, and can send any number of D2H Data
FISs. No assumption is made.
For a DMA data-out transfer, the transport layer will receive a D2H Dma_Activate FIS from the device. In the meantime the DMA engine
will pass data to the MUX unit by asserting vsMDdgive. The transport layer indicates to the MUX unit that the data it has should be
given to the link layer by asserting vsTMfisstart.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 186/242
=== PAGE 187 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
25.1.2.3.4 AHCI CFIS Cycle Walkthrough
Transport layer initiates an H2D Reg FIS transmission when the complete CFIS content is in the CFIS storage of transport layer and
vsDTstartdma is asserted. The mechanism is similar to that of writing to the legacy Command Register of the ATA taskfile. The
difference is that the transport layer need not separately generate the H2D Reg FIS header to the MUX unit since the header is part of
the content fetched from memory (initiated by the DMA Engine). The entire CFIS fetched from memory is provided via BMU to the
transport layer CFIS storage.
If the H2D Reg FIS gets an R_ERR from the SATA device, the transport layer will resend the H2D Reg FIS, using the same content from
CFIS storage, as many times as it takes to have an R_OK from the device.
In queued operation, the transport layer should temporarily stall a pending CFIS transmission, if a DMA Setup FIS is received. The
transport layer needs to wait until the entire data transfer has been completed before reattempting the CFIS transmission. An indication
from DMA that all data have been transferred in either direction can be used by the transport layer to start off the CFIS transmission.
25.1.2.3.5 AHCI DMA Cycle Walkthrough
In an AHCI DMA operation, both a DMA read or write starts off with a CFIS fetch and an H2D Reg FIS tranmission. After the H2D Reg FIS
transmission, the handling of the Data FIS in either direction is similar to that of a legacy DMA.
25.1.2.3.6 AHCI PIO Cycle Walkthrough
Both read and write AHCI PIO operations are handled identically to AHCI DMA operations since AHCI PIO data is also transferred via
DMA instead of CPU initiated IO cycle. The only exceptions being CFIS transmission and the D2H PIO_Setup FIS associated with a PIO
operation.
An AHCI PIO operation, read or write, starts off with CFIS transmission. Upon reception of D2H PIO_Setup FIS, transport layer in AHCI
mode would handle both incoming D2H Data FIS or initiating H2D Data FIS transmission no differently from the legacy/AHCI DMA
operations. The same data path as DMA is reused for AHCI PIO, i.e. IOSF->BMU-> MUX unit’s syncFIFO-> link layer for PIO data-out, or
link layer -> syncFIFO-> BMU->IOSF for PIO data-in.
PIO Read (data-in) operation does not have a final D2H Reg FIS and instead the Ending-Status in the Pio_Setup FIS reflects the final
status of a sata device. The transport layer needs to indicate to the DMA Engine on the timing to update the PxTFD with the content of
Ending-Status. Note that an independant logic must be created for updating Pio_Setup FIS Ending Status and not sharing the logic used
in the legacy mode.
25.1.2.3.7 AHCI BIST FIS
An AHCI BIST could be handled no differently from an AHCI CFIS transmission. Just like CFIS, the BIST FIS header is also part of the
memory content fetched by the DMA Engine. The transport layer still needs to inform the link layer (vsTL_BistFISxmitted) of a BIST FIS
transmission so that the link layer sends four(4) ALIGN primitives instead of two (2), as required by the Serial ATA1.0 spec. The
transport layer is able to distinguish a BIST FIS transmission from a CFIS transmission from the DMA Engine’s vsDTbist_bbit. This
indication is qualified by vsDTstartdma.
25.1.2.3.8 AHCI ATAPI Cycle Walkthrough
An AHCI ATAPI transmission is handled similarly to an AHCI PIO data-out operation. SATA IP made an implementation choice of
prefetching all 32-byte ATAPI data from memory space (initiated by the DMA Engine), even though in the ATAPI5/6 spec, only 16-bytes
of data from memory space would actually be sent to the SATA ATAPI device.
An AHCI ATAPI starts off with a CFIS transmission to the SATA ATAPI device, as an H2D Reg FIS. The CFIS content would be an ATAPI
Packet command (A0h), as defined in the ATA/ATAPI spec. In the mean time, the 32-byte ATAPI memory content is fetched and stored
in the syncFIFO. The actual amount to be transferred to the SATA ATAPI device is determined from the Transfer Count within the D2H
Pio_Setup FIS, corresponding to the H2D Reg FIS sent earlier.
The transport layer and the MUX unit is informed by the DMA Engine that the current operation is an ATAPI by tagging the ATAPI Packet
data before it goes into syncFIFO. The MUX unit pre-calculates a 32-byte offset from the current syncFIFO read pointer prior to storing
any ATAPI command in the syncFIFO. The transport layer initiates Data FIS following the D2H Pio_Setup FIS. When the transfer count in
the transport layer reaches zero, the MUX unit is informed and the MUX unit can advance the syncFIFO read pointer to the pre-
calculated read pointer with 32-byte offset. If the Pio_Setup FIS Transfer Count is less than 32 bytes, the remaining data in the syncFIFO
is invalidated automatically when the read pointer is advanced to the new pre-calculated value.
The syncFIFO read/write pointers cannot simply be reset, because the actual ATAPI data-out content could have been fetched by the
DMA Engine and stored in the syncFIFO.
25.1.2.3.9 AHCI RxFIS Handling
In AHCI mode, the following D2H FISes are stored in the RxFIS storage of the transport layer. The RxFIS type is provided to the DMA
Engine for posting to memory and certain contents of the RxFIS are steered from the RxFIS storage to the DMA Engine for register bit
updates or for starting queue operations. Table: Different Types Of AHCI RxFIS Handling shows the FIS received, the signal indicating
which RxFIS, as well as specific content being steered to the DMA Engine.

25.1.2.3.10 AHCI Device Reset
In AHCI mode, a CFIS transmission with the corresponding “R” bit (in the Command Header) triggers a SYNC escape mechanism, if
necessary, prior to CFIS transmission. The transport layer is made aware of the special CFIS that contains the DEVICE_RESET
command (08h), if the DMA Engine asserts vsDTreset_rbit (qualified by vsDTstartdma).
25.1.2.3.11 AHCI Queued Command
Transport layer supports a First Party DMA Setup FIS, as defined in the Serial ATA II spec. Command queuing starts off with multiple
CFIS transmissions (in the form of an H2D Reg FIS), and the each CFIS transmission is followed by a D2H Register FIS from the device
(atomic operation). The DMA Engine only initiates a new CFIS fetch after the AHCI. PxTFD.BSY and DRQ bit have been cleared by the
D2H Reg FIS and the atomic operation is enforced.
After one or more queue commands have been accepted by the device, the device can send a First Party DMA Setup FIS at any time
that there is no pending D2H Reg FIS. Upon reception of a DMA Setup FIS, in addition to storing the FIS into RxFIS storage, the tag
specified in the FIS is steered from the RxFIS storage to the DMA Engine as vsTDfpdmatag[4:0].
Based on the tag, the DMA Engine proceeds to fetch the command and get ready for data transfer.
From the transport layer perspective, the following FIS transaction that is involved in the entire queued operation is always atomic. The
only FIS that need not be atomic is the Set Device Bits FIS. For the entire queued operation, the device can send as many Set Device Bit
FISes as the number of First DMA Setup FISes or less, or even simply one Set Device Bit FIS at the end of entire queued operation to
clear all PxDSTS bits.
1. H2D Reg FIS
H2D Reg FIS
D2H Reg FIS
{another H2D Reg FIS or First Party DMA Setup FIS or SDB FIS}
1. First Party DMA Setup FIS (FPDMA)

Data FIS (more than one if >8KB) (either D2h or H2D)

25.1.2.3.12 AHCI SNotification FIS
The SATA IP supports the SNotification FIS (SDB-N) from a device behind a Port Multiplier in AHCI mode (see the Port Multiplier Section
of the SATA Specification). SDB-N is SDB FIS with bit-N(bit-15) set in the header. SDB-N with I-bit set will cause the host to set
PxIS.SDBS and generate an interrupt (if enabled via PxIE) and update the PxSNTF memory register. When the software is notified, it will
send a command to that drive as acknowledged. Device will not send another SNotification before it receives the software
acknowledgement. A different drive behind the Port Multiplier, or the Port Multiplier itself, can send an SNotification to the host. Hence,
the host needs to set the respective PxSNTF bit based on the PMP field. The generated interrupt may be overlapped.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 188/242
=== PAGE 189 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
25.1.2.4 VSAUNIT - Link
Figure: High Level Link Layer Data Path
The purpose of the link layer is to accept a FIS for transmission from the MUX unit, and frame the transmission with the SOF primitive,
EOF primitive, and CRC dword. During reception, the link layer will strip the SOF, EOF, and CRC, and present the raw FIS data to the MUX
unit and the transport layer. The link layer also verifies the CRC and indicates pass/fail status to both the device and to the transport
layer. In addition, the link layer allows the transport layer to fail the FIS received, and to cause the link layer to send an RERR primitive for
the received FIS during the handshake phase, even though the CRC is correct. The link layer also performs scrambling, descrambling,
ALIGN sequence insertion, 8B/10B encoding, 8B/10B decoding, CONT primitive insertion and deletion, and running disparity generation
and verification. The link layer also sequences the partial/slumber entry and exit sequences, interfaces to the Phy Control Logic for OOB
tranmission sequencing, and to the Phy Control logic to perform the calibration sequencing. The link layer also enters a far-end
loopback mode when commanded by the transport layer (upon receipt of the BIST_Activate FIS).
The link layer is not cognizant of the FIS type being transmitted and received. No FIS type decode is done here. All FIS contents are
parsed by the transport layer.
The link layer consists of a transmit data path (vsLx), receive data path (vsLr), main FSM (vsLf), and some shared logic used for both
transmit and receive (vsLs).
The data path is pipelined. A pipelined data path was chosen so that the frequency of the link layer will scale without a major micro-
architecture change.
There is a transmit pipeline and a receive pipeline. The transmit pipeline moves whenever the tx asyncFIFO has space to absorb another
dword. This is indicated by !txfifo_full. Otherwise the transmit pipeline will freeze, ithat is, all the pipeline flops simply recirculate their
values. The receive pipeline moves whenever the rx asyncFIFO has a dword ready at its output, as indicated by !rxfifo_empty. Otherwise,
the receive pipeline flops will re-circulate their values.

VSALC The clock crossing fub that crosses core-clock domain signals into the link clock (Lclk) domain.
25.1.3 PLUNIT and PLPUNIT
The physical layer or PHY logic controller (PLunit) manages the Serial ATA Analog Front End (AFE) circuits and is responsible for
establishing a point-to-point connection with a device.
The PLunit is partitioned into two major sections, port-specific and shared. Port-specific logic is located within the functional block
PLPunit. Adding control for a Serial ATA port is easy because each port requires one instantiation of the PLPunit block and each block
is guaranteed not to interact with another.
The PLPunit block is responsible for the following operations:
Power management of the individual port Serial ATA AFE circuits
Host Power-on Sequence, including speed negotiation
Out-of-Band signal detection
Out-of-Band signal generation
Communication of link status to the link-layer
Shared logic, as the name implies, controls functions that all ports have in common. This logic is located within several functional
blocks named PLautocal, PLpllctrl, and PLmonport.
These blocks are at the same hierarchy level as the PLPunit.
In addition to these blocks, there is one additional block that provides reset generation.
This block provides the following operations:
Reset generation - port-specific and shared
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 190/242
=== PAGE 191 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
Power management of the Serial ATA PLL and BandGap circuits
Impedance and current compensation for all ports
25.1.4 Sideband Agent Interface
This section describes the IOSF Sideband message interface at the A-spec level. The VBunit has several main services:
Access to PCI config, memory or IO mapped address space
General virtual wire messages
Distribution of fuse and setID values
Implementation specific messaging (power management, etc)
Figure: Partition of VBunit FUBs shows the high level partitioning of VBunit FUBs.
Figure: Partition of VBunit FUBs
The VBunit provides several features that are summarized here:
Provided by the Sideband Endpoint (SBEBASE)
parameterized ingress queue depth
parameterized support for extended header
Provided by the Sideband Agent
parameterized enable for each supported ingress and egress message
parameterized enable for source ID check for ingress messages
parameterized enable for destination ID check for ingress messages
parameterized enable for SAI check for ingress messages
parameterized ingress message size

25.2 SoC Architecture
This section describes the architectural requirements in SoC imposed by the SIP SATA.
25.2.1 Primary Scalable Fabric (PSF)
The PSF that SATA hook up to serves as the interconnect fabric between the multiple SATA IOSF primary agents (VRunit, one or more
VSunit). Refer to Figure: Connectivity between PSF and SATA SIP for the connectivity. VR and VS* are required to connect to the PSF
using non-buffered Fabric Port. All these Fabric Ports than connect to a single buffered Agent Port (shown in Figure: Connectivity
between PSF and SATA SIP) or buffered Fabric Port (not shown in Figure: Connectivity between PSF and SATA SIP). This is to ensure
the ordering requirement between VR and VS* as described in section Transaction Ordering Requirement.
Figure: Connectivity between PSF and SATA SIP
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 194/242
=== PAGE 195 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
This diagram shows 2 VSunits only.
Buffer sizes in PSF are configured to ensure the transactions are able to stream to meet the performance requirement described in
section Performance Requirement.
25.2.1.1 Performance Requirement
The five tables (per SoC) (Table: PSF Fabric Port Minimum Q depth Per SATA SIP Design/Cspec, Table: PSF Fabric Port Performance

and SoC Fabric (Upstream) per IOSF Spec) show the IOSF credit and the performance requirement of the PSF on both its Fabric port
and the Agent port. This information, plus the PSF architecture information, are able to tell the SoC what Q-depth to program to on the
PSF.
This information is not included in this HAS, so refer to the latest PSF HAS e.g. NVP_S at
(https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/NVL/PCH/HAS/Chap07_NVL_PCH_PSF/Chap07_NVL_PCH_PSF_HAS.html).

25.2.1.2 Transaction Ordering Requirement
25.2.1.2.1 Overview
IOSF does not specify any transaction ordering rules between channels with different QIDs, or between different agents.
IOSF requires transactions to be granted in request order for transactions with the same QID.
SATA SIP has multiple IOSF agents, therefore there is no transaction ordering rules between the multiple agents per IOSF.
This IOSF definition breaks the SATA SIP requirements that there be a transaction ordering between the multiple agents.
On both downstream and upstream, SATA SIP defines 1 channel per agent. The defined QID for each agent is listed in Table: SATA SIP
Defined QID, from Structure Perspective.
Table: SATA SIP Defined QID, from Structure Perspective (source)
Agent Channel Downstream Upstream
N/A N/A P NP CPL P NP CPL
VR 0 Yes Yes N/A Yes N/A Yes
VS# 0 Yes Yes Yes Yes Yes Yes
Legend: “Yes” means defined, “-” means not defined.
SATA SIP requires the PSF that it hook up to, to treat the multiple agents as a single agent, logically, for the sake of ordering, as
illustrated in Table: SATA SIP Defined Logical QID, from an Ordering Perspective, despite the fact that they are, in fact, multiple agents,
physically. This means:
The upstream posted cycles from the multiple agents share the same QID
The upstream non-posted cycles from the multiple agents share the same QID
The upstream completion cycles from the multiple agents share the same QID
The downstream posted cycles to the multiple agents share the same QID
The downstream non-posted cycles to the multiple agents share the same QID
The downstream completion cycles to the multiple agents share the same QID
Table: SATA SIP Defined Logical QID, from an Ordering Perspective (source)
Downstream Upstream
Agent Channel
P NP CPL P NP CPL
VR, VS# 0 Yes Yes Yes Yes Yes Yes
25.2.1.2.2 SATA SIP Transaction Ordering Rules
Refer to Table: Downstream Transaction Ordering Rules. Since PSF has more than 1 physical agent, there can be concurrent or
sequential req_put from SATA SIP to the PSF’s fabric ports. SATA SIP imposes extra transaction ordering rules on all the ingress req_put
and egress req_put of the PSF.
On the downstream, there can be concurrent or sequential cmd_puts from the PSF’s fabric ports to the SATA SIP. SATA SIP imposes
extra transaction ordering rules on all the ingress cmd_puts and egress cmd_puts of the PSF.
These transaction ordering rules are listed in Table: Downstream Transaction Ordering Rules and Table: Upstream Transaction Ordering
Rules.
Table: Downstream Transaction Ordering Rules (source)
Rule Description
Multiple cmd_puts entering the PSF that target different SATA SIP agents are treated as if they target the same SATA SIP agent and have
DR1
transaction ordering requirements between them per the “IOSF Request to Transaction Grant Ordering Rules Summary Table” in the IOSF Spec.
Even though there is more than 1 cmd_put signal to SATA SIP, these cmd_put signals are mutually exclusive, where only one can assert
DR2 at a time. This behavior is actually a side effect of rule DR1.
Additionally, if there are 2 cmd_puts with data, the 2nd cmd_put will occur at the last data phase of the 1st cmd_put.
Table: Upstream Transaction Ordering Rules (source)
Rule Description
UR1 Multiple gnts will not be asserted to different SATA SIP agent at the same time
A gnt to a SATA SIP agent followed by a req_put from another SATA SIP agent are treated as if they come from the same SATA SIP agent and
UR2
have transaction ordering requirements between them per the “IOSF Request to Transaction Grant Ordering Rules Summary Table.”
UR3 A req_put from a different SATA SIP agent that has not been granted has no ordering requirement between them.
Examples
To show examples of applying both IOSF rules and SATA SIP rules on transaction ordering, the Table: IOSF Request to Transaction
Grant Ordering Rules Summary Table in the IOSF spec is included here as reference. If there is a difference between references, use the
one from the IOSF specification.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 196/242

25.2.2 Fabric Decode
The SATA Fabric Decoding table in this HAS section has no notion of the Cycle Router, that is, it is only applicable in the product
without the Cycle Router. For product that has Cycle Router, then the Fabric Decoding table in this HAS section is not applicable and
Integration should refer to the SATA Fabric Decode table in NVM Remapping HAS.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 197/242
=== PAGE 198 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
The SATA SIP IOSF primary bus end point supports target decode for the downstream cycle targeting it.
If the SoC does not use the target decode, then part of the SoC needs to support the fabric decode to route the respective downstream
cycle to SATA SIP.
This section proposes a Fabric Decode Architecture for the SoC to implement the fabric decode for SATA SIP.
The basic idea of this architecture is to implement a shadow configuration table in the SoC fabric decoder (refer to sections Shadow
Configuration Table, and Other Definitions) and then use it to decide if a downstream cycle needs to route to SATA SIP or not (refer to
section Downstream Cycles Targeting SATA SIP).
The shadow configuration table is built by snooping the downstream cycle that targets SATA SIP, SATA SIP-related fuses and SATA SIP-
related straps.
Table: Naming Conventions (source)
Name Convention
For example: Config.PTIM.DE
This is an internal register of SATA. Refer to section 4. Interfaces.
Refer to SATA SIP Integration Guide (included in the ‘doc’ directory for this release). For more info, search for the ‘Register’ in the
Register:
correct space.
Space.Register.Field
Spaces are:
Config = Configuration
MMIO = Memory
Strap: For example: Strap.FunctionDisable
Strap.Name This is a signal input to SATA that is static.
Latched Value: For example: Latched.BusNumber
Latched.Name This is an internal register to SATA that SATA latches onto according to values that are presented to it.
X = don’t care, either 0 or 1
Values b = binary
h = hexadecimal
’*’ = AND
Symbols ‘!’ = NOT
‘&’ = concatenate

25.2.2.3 Other Definitions
Table: Other Definitions for Fabric Decode (source)
No Item Definition
FLR RESET occurs when the Config.FLR Control.Initiate FLR = 1 register writes for 1 clock.
When FLR RESET occurs, all physically shadowed registers that can be reset by FLR RESET are to be reset to the default value.
FLR
1 RESET The purpose of FLR RESET is to make sure that Source or Fabric Decode shadowed registers are in sync with actual SATA SIP
registers.
This flow ensures that all cycles (except Configuration cycles) that come after the FLR RESET will not be routed to SATA SIP.
SATA SIP is able to handle downstream cycles as Unsupported Requests when FLR is in progress.
SATA SIP is able to handle downstream cycles as Unsupported Requests when D3 is in progress. However, D3 register shadowing
can still be performed, as outlined in the decode sections above, depending on implementation choice.
25.2.3 Performance Estimation
This section provides the basics of the SATA SIP achievable performance with certain assumptions made about the device driver,
device, and the SoC to which it talks.
Example
The example scenario is AHCI 32 CI, where each CI is a DMA Data-In of 8K transfer using 1 PRD.
Assume:
The memory latency is 1 μs and device response is ideally fast (that is, that RH2D → DD2H, and the DD2H → RD2H is immediate)
Then the expected throughput is ~86% * 300MB/s = 260 MB/s per CI on average. The time frame is from when the 32 CI bits are set
until all of the 32 bits CI bits are cleared.
The throughput is reduced in real HDD, as the response time is much longer (as opposed to immediate in example above).
The throughput is reduced/increased with smaller/bigger transfer size (vs 8K in example above), as the overhead of the non-DATA DW
on wire and the memory fetch overhead is the same.
The throughput is reduced if more PRDs are used and the PRD size is <300B
(assume 1 μs of memory fetch latency).
25.2.4 IOSF Sideband Services
25.2.4.1 Services Overview
25.2.4.1.1 SoC Integration Rules
1. The SoC provides the 8-bit STSB_PORTID_ST strap to SIP to communicate the source port ID for the SIPgenerated message.
2. The SoC provides all the 8-bit destination port ID values to SIP to communicate the end-point-specific messages generated by SIP.
3. The SoC provides all the 8-bit opcodes values to SIP to communicate the end-point-specific messages generated by SIP.
4. The SoC provides all the 8-bit opcode values to SIP to communicate the end-point-specific messages received by SIP.
5. The SoC provides all the values needed for the Ingress message security check, such as the 8-bit SrcID, 8-bit DestID, and 24-bit SAI
code.
6. On the SIP ingress port, only the defined messages are claimed and handled as successful. A defined message means the attributes
listed below use the definitions provided in section SoC Integration Rules:
Opcode
DW # (excluding the Expended Header(s))
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 202/242
=== PAGE 203 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
P/C/NP
Register decode, for register access message only
Rsp, for completion message only
3-bit Tag, for completion message only
8-bit SrcID, message and SoC dependent
8-bit DstID, message and SoC dependent
24-bit SAI code, message and SoC dependent
bb_rst_b=1, message and SoC dependent
7. An unclaimed ingress NP cycle URs by the SIP
8. An unclaimed ingress PC cycle is dropped by the SIP. Examples of UR:
An ingress CPLD/CPL with 2 EH and SAI code matching is needed.
The EH-SAI matches the SAI code and if the second EH is not EH-SAI, then the SATA SIP claims the message.
An ingress CPLD/CPL with 2 EH and SAI code matching is needed.
If both EH-SAI match the SAI code, then SATA SIP drops the message because the SAI is limited to 1 EH per message, per the IOSF
specification.
9. Ingress Register access is limited to 16-bit address and 1 DW data.
No support of 48-bit address or 2 DW data. If a 48-bit address request or 2 DW request is received, the cycle is dropped and the
sideband interface proceeds without hanging.
In other words, an NP request causes the UR completion and credit to be returned, while a P request causes the credit to be returned.
10. For messages that resemble the legacy wire, the SoC needs to comprehend the requirements in sections Egress Messages and
Ingress Messages. Search for keyword Requirement to SoC.
The requirement is to avoid sending a message with a “default value”.

25.2.4.3 Services Operation
The operation of all the supported services are described in detail in this section.
25.2.4.3.1 Fuse Pulling
Participating Messages
Egress Fuse Request Message
Ingress Completion With Data Message
Ingress Completion Without Data Message
Egress IP Ready Message
Mechanism
Upon side_rst_b deassertion, SATA SIP automatically sends the NP Egress Fuse Request Message to the SoC. The SoC returns the fuse
completion by using the Ingress Completion With Data Message or Ingress Completion Without Data Message. Once the valid
completion is received, SATA SIP sends the P Egress IP Ready Message. The SoC is supposed to deassert the bb_rst_b after receiving
the Egress IP Ready Message from SIP.
The SoC completes the Fuse Pulling request with an Ingress Completion With Data Message if it wants SIP to update the fuse value
from its fuse box. (see Figure: SoC Updates Fuse Value to SIP)
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 216/242
=== PAGE 217 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
Figure: SoC Updates Fuse Value to SIP
If the SoC wants SIP to use the current fuse value, it complete the request by using an Ingress Completion Without Data Message. (See
Figure: SoC Requests SIP to Use its Current Fuse Value)
Figure: SoC Requests SIP to Use its Current Fuse Value
If the fuse Completion from the SoC has an error, the SIP uses the current fuse value and does not send the Egress IP Ready Message.
The SoC is expected to time-out and proceed to bb_rst_b deassertion to complete the boot process. (See Figure: Fuse Completion with
Error)
Figure: Fuse Completion with Error

fuse completion.

25.2.4.3.2 Mechanical Switch
Participating Messages
Ingress Mechanical Switch Message
Mechanism
The SoC sends this message to SIP when there is a rise or fall on the per-port SATA Device Mechanical Switch GP pin.
If SATA SIP runs in AHCI mode and the mechanical switch feature is supported, this message sets the corresponding register bits,
which trigger the interrupt, if the interrupt is enabled (See Figure: Mechanical Switch Message.)
Figure: Mechanical Switch Message
25.2.4.3.3 Thermal Throttling
Participating Messages
Ingress Thermal throttling Message
Mechanism
The SoC sends this message to SIP when there is a change of the TT Value. 4 T-states are defined in this message, where T0 means no
thermal throttling, while T1, T2 and T3 mean throttling is required.
Thermal throttling can be disabled in SATA SIP by programming the multiplier for T1, T2, and T3 to “disabled.”
25.2.4.3.4 Reset Warn
Participating Messages
Ingress Platform Reset Warn NP Message
Egress Completion With Data
Egress Completion Without Data
Mechanism
If the SoC does not assert the side_rst_b together with the bb_rst_b, SATA SIP expects a Reset Warn message to be received prior to the
following 4 boundary scenarios:
1. NP Reset_Warn msg starts, then P msg starts, then NP Reset_Warn msg ends,
then P msg ends → P is blocked.
2. NP Reset_Warn msg starts, then P msg starts, then P msg ends,
then NP Reset msg ends → P is not blocked.
3. P msg starts, then NP Reset Warn msg starts, then P msg ends,
then NP Reset Warn msg ends → P is not blocked.
4. P msg starts, then NP Reset Warn msg starts, then NP Reset Warn msg ends,
then P msg ends → P is not blocked.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 218/242
=== PAGE 219 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
Once a valid Reset Warn msg is received, it sets the SIP internal flag called $ResetWarnInProg. The $ResetWarnInProg is cleared when
side_rst_b asserts.
When $ResetWarnInProg=1, all received Posted messages are discarded except for case number 4 mentioned above. Non-Posted
messages are discarded and UR is returned.
Also, once the completion for a valid Reset Warn msg is returned, SIP does not send any more egress messages, except for the UR
completion as a response to the ingress NP msg, until $ResetWarnInProg is cleared.
25.2.4.3.5 Set ID
Participating Messages
Ingress SetID Message
Egress Completion With Data
Egress Completion Without Data
Mechanism
Sent by PMC or BIOS to update various ID value in SATA SIP config register. If it’s coming from BIOS, it happens after prim_rst_b
deassertion, hence BIOS needs to ensure that the completion of this message is returned to CPU prior to allowing next request from
CPU to access those ID value from IOSF primary bus, else the ID value onbtained from IOSF primary bus may be undefined.
25.2.4.3.6 SATA PCI Register Access
Participating Messages
Ingress MMIO Register Read Request
Ingress MMIO Register Write Request
Ingress IO Register Read Request
Ingress IO Register Write Request
Ingress CONFIG Register Read Request
Ingress CONFIG Register Write Request
Egress Completion With Data
Egress Completion Without Data
Mechanism
All PCI registers accessible by the Primary bus are accessible by the Sideband. Concurrent register access from both the Primary bus
and Sideband bus is prohibited. However there is a simple arbiter in-placed to prevent the bus from hanging. If concurrent access
occurs, the SIP causes a UR on the loser bus.
For example, if the Sideband bus completes an NP read to the Legacy IO taskfile register and then the Primary bus completes an NP
read to the config register, the Primary bus then receives a UR completion.
Register access during bb_rst_b=0 is prohibited, but if this happens, the SIP gracefully handles it by returing a UR for NP and drops the
data (if it is a write message). For a P message, the data is dropped. The SIP endpoint returns the credit accordingly. (See Figure: PCI
Register Access Messages)
Figure: PCI Register Access Messages
Section Decode Table describes the claim conditions for access to all of the register space. This condition is part of the set of
conditions mentioned in section Ingress Messages.
25.2.4.3.6.1 Decode Table
Table: Naming Conventions, Table: IOSF Sideband Register Access DECODING–BAR0, BAR1, BAR2, and BAR4, and Table: IOSF
Sideband Register Access DECODING–BAR5 show the conditions for a VRunit to claim a register access from the Sideband interface.
This VRunit condition is an add-on to the condition mentioned in the register-access sideband messages in section Ingress Messages.

25.2.4.3.7 Wire Interrupt Reporting
Participating Messages
Egress PCI Wire Interrupt Message
Egress Legacy Wire Interrupt Message
vrintB1
There is one interrupt signal generated from SATA SIP: vrintB1.
This interrupt signal is sent over to the SoC Interrupt Controller via the IOSF Sideband. The vrintB1 is the wire interrupt for the whole
SATA SIP. Both the sourceID and destinationID are provided by the SoC via the input strap.
The vrintB1 is the local PCI (that is, native) interrupt level in SATA SIP and meant for level trigger (and possibly edge trigger) interrupt by
the SoC Interrupt Controller to the CPU. It is sent to the SoC Interrupt Controller via the IOSF Sideband by using the 8 defined PCI
interrupt opcodes defined in the IOSF Spec 1.1
Either INTA or INTB or INTC or INTD is used depending on the “Interrupt Pin” input strap from the SoC.
Per the PCI Local Bus Spec rev2.2, the “Interrupt Pin” input strap is defined as follows:
1 means INTA, 2 means INTB, 3 means INTC and 4 means INTD. 0 means no interrupt and other values are reserved.
SoC HW and SW ensure that the “Interrupt Pin” is driving 1 or 2 or 3 or 4 if it programs SATA to use the PCI interrupt.
If SoC HW drives 0 or a reserved value on the “Interrupt Pin” and the SW programs SATA to use the PCI interrupt, the behavior is
undefined.
Table: Opcodes for vrintB1 Messages.
Opcode Symbol Type Description
8h’80 Assert_INTA Posted Assert INTA virtual wire
8h’81 Assert_INTB Posted Assert INTB virtual wire
8h’82 Assert_INTC Posted Assert INTC virtual wire
8h’83 Assert_INTD Posted Assert INTD virtual wire
8h’84 Deassert_INTA Posted Deassert INTA virtual wire
8h’85 Deassert_INTB Posted Deassert INTB virtual wire
8h’86 Deassert_INTC Posted Deassert INTC virtual wire
8h’87 Deassert_INTD Posted Deassert INTD virtual wire
Upon platform reset (bb_rst_b) deassertion, SATA and the SoC Interrupt Controller reset their local vrintB1 to level 1 to mean no
interrupt.
SATA allows the vrintB1 message to be sent over the IOSF Sideband after SW enables the native interrupt via the SATA Config register.
The SoC Interrupt Controller reconstructs the vrintB1 and uses it to trigger the interrupt to the CPU.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 223/242
=== PAGE 224 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
Figure: vrintB1 Behavior over the IOSF Sideband
25.2.4.3.8 PME Reporting
Participating Messages
Egress Wire PME Message
Mechanism
Sent when there is a rise or fall event on the PME_Status in SATA Controller config space.
25.2.4.3.9 CPPM LTR Reporting
Participating Messages
Egress CPPM LTR Message
Mechanism
LTR message is lossy, either triggered by HW events or SW overriding events.
25.2.4.3.10 DO_SERR Reporting
Participating Messages
Egress DO_SERR Message
Mechanism
SATA SIP sends this message when any of the three events below happen:
When there is a set event to the config bits SATAGC.URD=1, during SATAGC.URRE=1 and CMD.SEE=1.
When there is a set event to the config bits STS.DPE=1 during CMD.PEE=1 and CMD.SEE=1.
When there is a set event to the config bits STS.STA=1, during CND.SEE=1.
25.2.4.3.11 SATA LED Reporting
Participating Messages
Egress SATA LED Message
Mechanism
The SATA LED Message is sent by SATA SIP when there is a rise/fall event on its internal wire LED. It is possible that the number of LED
Messages sent is less than the total number of internal wire LEDs toggling, due to the Sideband interface message sending a delay. In
other words, the LED Message could be lossy. Nevertheless, the SATA SIP always sends the very first and last message.
Figure: SATA LED Message is Lossy shows an example of an LED ON message dropped and an LED OFF message merged with the
previous one.
Figure: SATA LED Message is Lossy
25.2.5 Low Power OOB Detector
The SATA SIP has the low-power OOB detector run in low-frequency (25 MHz or 19.2 MHz) to detect the OOB event (COMWAKE or
COMINIT) from the device.
On the transmitter side, the valid burst transmit length defined in SATA specification is
103.5ns ≤T ≤109.9ns.
On the receiver side, the SATA specification defines the gap detection window (Table: COMWAKE/COMINIT Gap Detection Windows)
to validate/invalidate an OOB event but not the burst detection window.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 224/242
=== PAGE 225 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
The SATA SIP has an implementation-specific specification on the burst detection window (Table: COMWAKE/COMINIT Burst Detection
Windows). The purpose is to reject an OOB if the burst is too long.
This feature can be turned off.
Table: COMWAKE/COMINIT Burst Detection Windows (source)
Parameter Units Limit Electrical Specification(on SATA RXp/n)
COMWAKE / COMINIT Burst Can Detect 120 ≤ T ≤ 220
Detection ns Detects T < 120
Windows Does Not Detect T > 220
Table: COMWAKE/COMINIT Gap Detection Windows (source)
Parameter Units Limit Electrical Specification(on SATA RXp/n)
COMWAKE Gap Can Detect 35 ≤ T < 175
Detection ns Detects 101.3 ≤ T ≤ 112
Windows Does Not Detect 35 < T or T ≥ 175
COMINIT Gap Can Detect 175 ≤ T < 525
Detection ns Detects 304 ≤ T ≤ 336
Windows Does Not Detect 175 < T or T ≥ 525
The low power OOB detector uses a lower sampling frequency as well as both edges of the sosc_clk to sample. The requirements that
support this feature for the SoC and MOD-PHY are listed in sections Requirements for SoC and Requirements for MOD-PHY.
25.2.5.1 Requirements for SoC
sosc_clk duty cycle variation + uncertainty <2 ns.
sosc_clk path + ilane#rxellecidle paths’ timing skew to both +po edge and -ne edge dsync of the new Low Power OOB Detector is <2
ns.
Figure: Requirements for SoC
25.2.5.2 Requirements for MOD-PHY
ilane#rxellecidle is analog output subjected to Squelch shrinkage without further signal quality deterioration.
Squelch shrinkage:
≤13 ns (for sosc_clk=19.2 MHz) or ≤ 16 ns (for sosc_clk=25 MHz or higher).
25.2.6 20 DW HOLD-HOLDA Latency
Per the SATA specification:
The maximum allowed latency from the time the MSB of the initial HOLD is on the wire, until the MSB of the initial HOLDA is on the wire
should be no more than 20 Dword times. The LSB is transmitted first.
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 225/242
=== PAGE 226 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
HOLD-HOLDA latency can be divided into 5 segments, as shown in Table: HOLD-HOLDA Latency Segments and Figure: HOLD-HOLDA
Latency Segments.
Table: HOLD-HOLDA Latency Segments
Segment Description Examples
Ta Latency of the HOLD MSB at the Device TX pad to the HOLD MSB at the Host AFE RX pad Wire Delay
Tb Latency of the HOLD MSB at the AFE RX pad to the HOLD MSB at the Link RX pin PCS/AFE RX Delay
Tc Latency of the HOLD MSB at the Link RX pin to the HOLDA MSB at the Link Tx pin Link Delay
Td Latency of the HOLDA MSB at the Link TX pin to the HOLDA MSB at the AFE TX pad P CS/AFE TX Delay
Te Latency of the HOLDA MSB at the AFE TX pad to the HOLDA MSB at the Device RX pad Wire Delay
Figure: HOLD-HOLDA Latency Segments
25.2.6.1 Link Delay Tc Calculation in Spatial Domain
The purpose of this analysis is to conclude that the link delay is 10.6 DW in the worse case, across all possible Pclk/Lclk relationships
by using spatial domain analysis with:
Pclk = 300 MH @ 20-bit and Lclk = 250 MHz @ 40-bit.
Spatial domain analysis here means that knowing the total number of flop stages between the HOLD reception and the HOLDA
transmission, we can tell the latency between them.
If there is only 1 clock domain in the Link layer, the number of flop stages is straight forward.But, when it involves clock crossing,
convert the time domain info into a flop stage.
The following sections show this conversion from time domain to spatial domain for both RX FIFO and TX FIFO.
Architectural assumption in this analysis:
Link Lclk frequency is a variable
Link Lclk data bus width is fixed at 40-bit DW
Link Pclk frequency is a variable
Link Pclk data bus width is a variable
Link TX FIFO width is fixed at 40-bit DW
Link TX FIFO depth is a variable
Link RX FIFO width is fixed at 40-bit DW
Link RX FIFO depth is a variable
https://docs.intel.com/documents/pch_doc/CLIP/SATA/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller/Chap25_SATA_Host_Controller.html 226/242
=== PAGE 227 ===
3/16/26, 4:16 PM SATA 3.0 Controller
Figure: Link Delay Tc
25.2.6.2 RX Async FIFO
The worse case occurs when HOLD MSB is entering the RX FIFO and the “previously stored data” are not yet consumed but are about to
be consumed by rxstage1, where the 1st DW of this “previously stored data” enters the RX FIFO when the RX FIFO is empty. This means,
as much data as possible is in the RX FIFO before a HOLD enters.
Because a HOLD is received in the middle of DATA FIS, but not at the end of OOB, this means that the RX FIFO empty condition already
happens much earlier than when the HOLD is received, and so, we do not hit this worse case. Lets assumed we can achieve the worst
case here.
The question asked is how much “previously stored data” is there?
It should be 1 + (SATA_BW * (3 Lclk) / 4) DW.
This means that after 1 DW is written into the FIFO, during the subsequent 3 Lclks of time, the write pointer is allowed to be cross-
clocked to the read domain, and there will be more bytes written to the FIFO.
This 1 DW plus the additional bytes are the so-called “previously stored data.”
Table: Components Breakdown (source)
Component Description
1 The 1st DW written the RX FIFO when the RX FIFO empty.
3 Lclk 2 clocks to double sync the rptr in the longest possible time, and 1 clock for consumption by the wptr, in ns.
SATA_BW The RX FIFO inlet throughput, i.e. the SATA bandwidth, in GB/s.
4 Convert from byte to DW.
RX FIFO depth is not important in this calculation.
Table: RX Async FIFO (source)

25.2.6.3 TX Async FIFO
The worse case is when HOLDA MSB enters the TX FIFO to fill up the DW that has just been consumed by the PCS TX Flop, and this DW
is consumed by the PCS TX Flop when the TX FIFO is FULL, that is, with as much data as possible in the TX FIFO before HOLDA enter.
Since HOLDA is transmitted in the middle of the DATA FIS, but not at the end of OOB, that means the TX FIFO full condition already
happened much earlier than when HOLDA was transmitted, therefore we will not hit this worse case. We assumed we can achieve the
worst case here.
The question is not how much will the TX FIFO have when it is full, because that answer is straight forward–equal to the TX FIFO depth.
The question is how much data is there that is still in the queue in front of HOLDA.
It should be TXFIFO_DEPTH - 1 - (SATA_BW * (2 Lclk) / 4) DW. This means that after 1 DW is just read from the FIFO, and during the
subsequent 2 Lclk of time to cross clock this DW’s rptr to the write domain and consumed, there are some more bytes being read from
the FIFO.
The 1 DW plus some more bytes together are the so-called “data that is still queued in front of the HOLDA”.
Table: Components Breakdown (source)
Component Description
TXFIFO_DEPTH Depth of the TX FIFO. 4 DW for 2010 SATA SIP.
1 The 1st DW consumed by the PCS TX Flop when the TX FIFO is full.
2 Lclk 1 clock to double sync the rptr in the shortest possible time, and 1 clock for consumption by the wptr, in ns.
SATA_BW The TX FIFO outlet throughput, i.e. the SATA bandwidth, in GB/s.
4 Convert from byte to DW.
